<td colspan="3"><small>Remnants of the Ming dynasty ruled southern China until 1662, and Taiwan until 1683 a dynastic period which is known as the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ming" title="Southern Ming">Southern Ming</a>.<br/>
¹The numbers are based on estimates made by CJ Peers in <i>Late Imperial Chinese Armies: 1520–1840</i><br/>
²According to A. G. Frank, <i>ReOrient: global economy in the Asian Age</i>, 1998, p. 109<br/>
³According to A. Maddison, <i>The World Economy Volume 1: A Millennial Perspective Volume 2</i>, 2007, p. 238</small></td>

<td colspan="8" style="line-height: 125%; font-size: 80%; padding: 2px 2px 2px 3px; border-top: 1px solid #777; background: #FEC; text-align:center;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><span style="word-spacing:0">This box:</span>
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_China" title="Template:History of China"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_China" title="Template talk:History of China"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_China&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li>

</td>

</table>
<p>The <b>Ming dynasty</b> was the <a href="/wiki/Dynasties_in_Chinese_history" title="Dynasties in Chinese history">ruling dynasty</a> of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>—then known as the <span class="nowrap"><b>Empire of the Great Ming</b></span>—for 276 years (1368–1644) following the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol</a>-led <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>. The Ming, described by some as "one of the greatest eras of orderly government and social stability in human history,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[2]</a></sup> was the last imperial dynasty in China ruled by ethnic <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a>. Although the primary capital of <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> fell in 1644 to a rebellion led by <a href="/wiki/Li_Zicheng" title="Li Zicheng">Li Zicheng</a> (who established the <a href="/wiki/Shun_dynasty" title="Shun dynasty">Shun dynasty</a>, soon replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Manchu</a>-led <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>), regimes loyal to the Ming throne – collectively called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Ming_dynasty" title="Southern Ming dynasty">Southern Ming</a> – survived until 1683.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a> (ruled 1368–98) attempted to create a society of self-sufficient rural communities ordered in a rigid, immobile system that would guarantee and support a permanent class of soldiers for his dynasty:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhangwen_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-zhangwen-6">[3]</a></sup> the empire's standing army exceeded one million troops and the <a href="/wiki/Naval_history_of_China" title="Naval history of China">navy</a>'s dockyards in Nanjing were the largest in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006271_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006271-7">[4]</a></sup> He also took great care breaking the power of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eunuch_(court_official)#China" title="Eunuch (court official)">court eunuchs</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eunuchpower_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-eunuchpower-8">[5]</a></sup> and unrelated magnates, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enfeoffment" title="Enfeoffment">enfeoffing</a> his many sons throughout China and attempting to guide these princes through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huang_Ming_Zu_Xun" title="Huang Ming Zu Xun">Huang Ming Zu Xun</a>, a set of published dynastic instructions. This failed spectacularly when his teenage successor, the <a href="/wiki/Jianwen_Emperor" title="Jianwen Emperor">Jianwen Emperor</a>, attempted to curtail his uncles' power, prompting the <a href="/wiki/Jingnan_Campaign" title="Jingnan Campaign">Jingnan Campaign</a>, an uprising that placed the Prince of Yan upon the throne as the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a> in 1402. The Yongle Emperor established Yan as a secondary capital and renamed it <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, constructed the <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">Forbidden City</a>, and restored the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_of_China" title="Grand Canal of China">Grand Canal</a> and the primacy of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keju" title="Keju">imperial examinations</a> in official appointments. He rewarded his eunuch supporters and employed them as a counterweight against the Confucian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-bureaucrats" title="Scholar-bureaucrats">scholar-bureaucrats</a>. One, <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a>, led seven enormous <a href="/wiki/Treasure_voyages" title="Treasure voyages">voyages of exploration</a> into the Indian Ocean as far as Arabia and the eastern coasts of Africa.</p>
<p>The rise of new emperors and new factions diminished such extravagances; the capture of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhengtong_Emperor" title="Zhengtong Emperor">Zhengtong Emperor</a> during the 1449 <a href="/wiki/Tumu_Crisis" title="Tumu Crisis">Tumu Crisis</a> ended them completely. The imperial navy was allowed to fall into disrepair while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corvee#Imperial_China" title="Corvee">forced labor</a> constructed the Liaodong palisade and connected and fortified the <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall of China</a> into its modern form. Wide-ranging censuses of the entire empire were conducted decennially, but the desire to avoid labor and taxes and the difficulty of storing and reviewing the enormous archives at Nanjing hampered accurate figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhangwen_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-zhangwen-6">[3]</a></sup> Estimates for the late-Ming population vary from 160 to 200 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[6]</a></sup> but necessary revenues were squeezed out of smaller and smaller numbers of farmers as more disappeared from the official records or "donated" their lands to tax-exempt eunuchs or temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhangwen_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-zhangwen-6">[3]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Haijin" title="Haijin">Haijin</a></i> laws intended to protect the coasts from <a href="/wiki/Wokou" title="Wokou">"Japanese" pirates</a> instead turned many into smugglers and pirates themselves.</p>
<p>By the 16th century, however, the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">expansion of European trade</a>—albeit restricted to islands near <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> like <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Macau" title="Portuguese Macau">Macau</a>—spread the <a href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">Columbian Exchange</a> of crops, plants, and animals into China, introducing <a href="/wiki/Chili_pepper" title="Chili pepper">chili peppers</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sichuan_cuisine" title="Sichuan cuisine">Sichuan cuisine</a> and highly productive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corn" title="Corn">corn</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potatoes" title="Potatoes">potatoes</a>, which diminished famines and spurred population growth. The growth of <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_Portugal#Triangular_trade_between_China.2C_Japan.2C_and_Europe" title="Economic history of Portugal">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_Spain#Gold_and_silver_from_the_New_World" title="Economic history of Spain">Spanish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_the_Netherlands_(1500%E2%80%931815)" title="Economic history of the Netherlands (1500–1815)">Dutch</a> trade created new demand for Chinese products and produced a massive influx of <a href="/wiki/Iwami_Ginzan_Silver_Mine" title="Iwami Ginzan Silver Mine">Japanese</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manila_galleons" title="Manila galleons">American</a> silver. This abundance of specie remonetized the Ming economy, whose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_currency" title="Ming currency">paper money</a> had suffered repeated <a href="/wiki/Hyperinflation" title="Hyperinflation">hyperinflation</a> and was no longer trusted. While traditional Confucians opposed such a prominent role for commerce and the newly rich it created, the heterodoxy introduced by <a href="/wiki/Wang_Yangming" title="Wang Yangming">Wang Yangming</a> permitted a more accommodating attitude. <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Juzheng" title="Zhang Juzheng">Zhang Juzheng</a>'s initially successful reforms proved devastating when a slowdown in agriculture produced by the <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a> joined changes in Japanese and Spanish policy that quickly cut off the supply of silver now necessary for farmers to be able to pay their taxes. Combined with crop failure, floods, and epidemic, the dynasty collapsed before the rebel leader <a href="/wiki/Li_Zicheng" title="Li Zicheng">Li Zicheng</a>, who was defeated by the Manchu-led <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eight_Banner" title="Eight Banner">Eight Banner</a> armies who founded the Qing dynasty.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Ming_dynasty" title="History of the Ming dynasty">History of the Ming dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_emperors_of_the_Ming_dynasty" title="List of emperors of the Ming dynasty">List of emperors of the Ming dynasty</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding">Founding</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolt_and_rebel_rivalry">Revolt and rebel rivalry</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol</a>-led <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> (1271–1368) ruled before the establishment of the Ming dynasty. Explanations for the demise of the Yuan include institutionalized ethnic discrimination against <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> that stirred resentment and rebellion, overtaxation of areas hard-hit by <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a>, and massive flooding of the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a> as a result of the abandonment of irrigation projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGascoigne2003150_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGascoigne2003150-10">[7]</a></sup> Consequently, agriculture and the economy were in shambles, and rebellion broke out among the hundreds of thousands of peasants called upon to work on repairing the dykes of the Yellow River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGascoigne2003150_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGascoigne2003150-10">[7]</a></sup> A number of Han Chinese groups revolted, including the <a href="/wiki/Red_Turban_Rebellion" title="Red Turban Rebellion">Red Turbans</a> in 1351. The Red Turbans were affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/White_Lotus" title="White Lotus">White Lotus</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism" title="Chinese Buddhism">Buddhist</a> secret society. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhu_Yuanzhang" title="Zhu Yuanzhang">Zhu Yuanzhang</a> was a penniless peasant and Buddhist monk who joined the Red Turbans in 1352; he soon gained a reputation after marrying the foster daughter of a rebel commander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999190.E2.80.931_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999190.E2.80.931-11">[8]</a></sup> In 1356, Zhu's rebel force captured the city of <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGascoigne2003151_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGascoigne2003151-12">[9]</a></sup> which he would later establish as the capital of the Ming dynasty.</p>
<p>With the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> crumbling, competing rebel groups began fighting for control of the country and thus the right to <a href="/wiki/Dynastic_cycle" title="Dynastic cycle">establish a new dynasty</a>. In 1363, Zhu Yuanzhang eliminated his archrival and leader of the rebel Han faction, <a href="/wiki/Chen_Youliang" title="Chen Youliang">Chen Youliang</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lake_Poyang" title="Battle of Lake Poyang">Battle of Lake Poyang</a>, arguably the <a href="/wiki/Largest_naval_battle_in_history" title="Largest naval battle in history">largest naval battle in history</a>. Known for its ambitious use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire_ships" title="Fire ships">fire ships</a>, Zhu's force of 200,000 Ming sailors were able to defeat a Han rebel force over triple their size, claimed to be 650,000-strong. The victory destroyed the last opposing rebel faction, leaving Zhu Yuanzhang in uncontested control of the bountiful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River_Valley" title="Yangtze River Valley">Yangtze River Valley</a> and cementing his power in the south. After the dynastic head of the Red Turbans suspiciously died in 1367 while a guest of Zhu, there was no one left who was remotely capable of contesting his march to the throne, and he made his imperial ambitions known by sending an army toward the Yuan capital <a href="/wiki/Khanbaliq" title="Khanbaliq">Dadu</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>) in 1368.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999191_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999191-13">[10]</a></sup> The last Yuan emperor fled north to the upper capital <a href="/wiki/Shangdu" title="Shangdu">Shangdu</a>, and Zhu declared the founding of the Ming dynasty after razing the Yuan palaces in Dadu to the ground;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999191_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999191-13">[10]</a></sup> the city was renamed Beiping in the same year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[11]</a></sup> Zhu Yuanzhang took Hongwu, or "Vastly Martial", as his <a href="/wiki/Chinese_era_name" title="Chinese era name">era name</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_of_the_Hongwu_Emperor">Reign of the Hongwu Emperor</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hongwu1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="609" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Hongwu1.jpg/170px-Hongwu1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Hongwu1.jpg/255px-Hongwu1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Hongwu1.jpg/340px-Hongwu1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hongwu1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait of the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a> (ruled in 1368–98)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hongwu made an immediate effort to rebuild state infrastructure. He built a 48 km (30 mi) long <a href="/wiki/City_Wall_of_Nanjing" title="City Wall of Nanjing">wall around Nanjing</a>, as well as new palaces and government halls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999191_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999191-13">[10]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Ming" title="History of Ming">History of Ming</a></i> states that as early as 1364 Zhu Yuanzhang had begun drafting a new <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucian</a> law code, the <i>Da Ming Lü</i>, which was completed by 1397 and repeated certain clauses found in the old <a href="/wiki/Tang_Code" title="Tang Code">Tang Code</a> of 653.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrewRapp200025_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrewRapp200025-15">[12]</a></sup> Hongwu organized a military system known as the <i>weisuo</i>, which was similar to the <a href="/wiki/Fubing_system" title="Fubing system"><i>fubing</i> system</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (618–907).</p>
<p>In 1380 Hongwu had the Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Hu_Weiyong" title="Hu Weiyong">Hu Weiyong</a> executed upon suspicion of a conspiracy plot to overthrow him; after that Hongwu abolished the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chancellor_(China)" title="Chancellor (China)">Chancellery</a> and assumed this role as chief executive and emperor, a precedent mostly followed throughout the Ming period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999192.E2.80.933_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999192.E2.80.933-16">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006130_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006130-17">[14]</a></sup> With a growing suspicion of his ministers and subjects, Hongwu established the <a href="/wiki/Jinyiwei" title="Jinyiwei">Jinyiwei</a>, a network of <a href="/wiki/Secret_police" title="Secret police">secret police</a> drawn from his own palace guard. Some 100,000 people were executed in a series of purges during his rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999192.E2.80.933_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999192.E2.80.933-16">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006129.E2.80.9330_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006129.E2.80.9330-18">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Hongwu emperor issued many edicts forbidding Mongol practices and proclaiming his intention to purify China of barbarian influence. However, he also sought to use the Yuan legacy to legitimize his authority in China and other areas ruled by the Yuan. He adopted many Yuan military practices, recruited Mongol soldiers, and continued to request Korean concubines and eunuchs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson2008367.E2.80.93368_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson2008367.E2.80.93368-19">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South-Western_frontier">South-Western frontier</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ming_conquest_of_Yunnan" title="Ming conquest of Yunnan">Ming conquest of Yunnan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dali-puerta-sur-c01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="651" data-file-width="1023" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Dali-puerta-sur-c01.jpg/220px-Dali-puerta-sur-c01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Dali-puerta-sur-c01.jpg/330px-Dali-puerta-sur-c01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Dali-puerta-sur-c01.jpg/440px-Dali-puerta-sur-c01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dali-puerta-sur-c01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The old south gate of the ancient city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dali,_Yunnan" title="Dali, Yunnan">Dali, Yunnan</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Qinghai" title="Qinghai">Qinghai</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Salar_people" title="Salar people">Salar</a> Muslims voluntarily came under Ming rule, their clan leaders capitulating around 1370. <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Qocho" title="Kingdom of Qocho">Uyghur</a> troops under Uyghur general Hala Bashi suppressed the <a href="/wiki/Miao_Rebellions_(Ming_dynasty)" title="Miao Rebellions (Ming dynasty)">Miao Rebellions</a> of the 1370s and settled in Changde, Hunan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harmony_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-harmony-20">[17]</a></sup> Hui Muslim troops also settled in Changde, Hunan after serving the Ming in campaigns against other aboriginal tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup> In 1381, the Ming dynasty annexed the areas of the southwest that had once been part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Dali" title="Kingdom of Dali">Kingdom of Dali</a> following the successful effort by Hui Muslim Ming armies to defeat <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan</a>-loyalist Mongol and Hui Muslim troops holding out in Yunnan province. The <a href="/wiki/Hui_people" title="Hui people">Hui</a> troops under General <a href="/wiki/Mu_Ying" title="Mu Ying">Mu Ying</a>, who was appointed Governor of Yunnan, were resettled in the region as part of a colonization effort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup> By the end of the 14th century, some 200,000 military colonists settled some 2,000,000 <i>mu</i> (350,000 acres) of land in what is now <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guizhou" title="Guizhou">Guizhou</a>. Roughly half a million more Chinese settlers came in later periods; these migrations caused a major shift in the ethnic make-up of the region, since formerly more than half of the population were non-Han peoples. Resentment over such massive changes in population and the resulting government presence and policies sparked more <a href="/wiki/Miao_people" title="Miao people">Miao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yao_people" title="Yao people">Yao</a> revolts in 1464 to 1466, which were crushed by an army of 30,000 Ming troops (including 1,000 Mongols) joining the 160,000 local <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Miao_Rebellions_(Ming_dynasty)" title="Miao Rebellions (Ming dynasty)">Miao Rebellions (Ming dynasty)</a>). After the scholar and philosopher <a href="/wiki/Wang_Yangming" title="Wang Yangming">Wang Yangming</a> (1472–1529) suppressed another rebellion in the region, he advocated single, unitary administration of Chinese and indigenous ethnic groups in order to bring about <a href="/wiki/Sinicization" title="Sinicization">sinification</a> of the local peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999197_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999197-23">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_in_the_North-East">Campaign in the North-East</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Manchuria_under_Ming_rule" title="Manchuria under Ming rule">Manchuria under Ming rule</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chemin_de_ronde_muraille_long.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Chemin_de_ronde_muraille_long.JPG/220px-Chemin_de_ronde_muraille_long.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Chemin_de_ronde_muraille_long.JPG/330px-Chemin_de_ronde_muraille_long.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Chemin_de_ronde_muraille_long.JPG/440px-Chemin_de_ronde_muraille_long.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chemin_de_ronde_muraille_long.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall of China</a>: Although the <a href="/wiki/Rammed_earth" title="Rammed earth">rammed earth</a> walls of the ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warring_States" title="Warring States">Warring States</a> were combined into a unified wall under the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a> dynasties, the vast majority of the brick and stone Great Wall seen today is a product of the Ming dynasty.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the overthrow of the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongol</a> <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> by the Ming dynasty in 1368, Manchuria remained under control of the Mongols of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Yuan_dynasty" title="Northern Yuan dynasty">Northern Yuan dynasty</a> based in <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>. <a href="/wiki/Naghachu" title="Naghachu">Naghachu</a>, a former Yuan official and a <a href="/wiki/Uriankhai" title="Uriankhai">Uriankhai</a> general of the Northern Yuan dynasty, won hegemony over the Mongol tribes in Manchuria (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liaoyang_province" title="Liaoyang province">Liaoyang province</a> of the former Yuan dynasty). He grew strong in the northeast, with forces large enough (numbering hundreds of thousands) to threaten invasion of the newly founded Ming dynasty in order to restore the Mongols to power in China. The Ming decided to defeat him instead of waiting for the Mongols to attack. In 1387 the Ming sent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_military_campaign_against_Naghachu" title="Ming military campaign against Naghachu">a military campaign to attack Naghachu</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup> which concluded with the surrender of Naghachu and Ming conquest of Manchuria.</p>
<p>The early Ming court could not, and did not, aspire to the control imposed upon the <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_people" title="Jurchen people">Jurchens</a> in Manchuria by the Mongols, yet it created a norm of organization that would ultimately serve as the principal vehicle for the relations with peoples along the northeast frontiers. By the end of the Hongwu reign, the essentials of a policy toward the Jurchens had taken shape. Most of the inhabitants of Manchuria, except for the wild Jurchens, were at peace with China. The Ming had created many guards (衛, wei) in Manchuria, but the creation of a guard did not necessarily imply political control. In 1409, the Ming dynasty under Yongle Emperor established the <a href="/wiki/Nurgan_Regional_Military_Commission" title="Nurgan Regional Military Commission">Nurgan Regional Military Commission</a> on the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Amur_River" title="Amur River">Amur River</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yishiha" title="Yishiha">Yishiha</a>, a eunuch of <a href="/wiki/Haixi_Jurchens" title="Haixi Jurchens">Haixi Jurchen</a> derivation, was ordered to lead an expedition to the mouth of the Amur to pacify the Wild Jurchens. After the death of Yongle Emperor, the Nurgan Regional Military Commission was abolished in 1435, and the Ming court ceased to have substantial activities there, although the guards continued to exist in Manchuria. By the late Ming period, Ming political presence in Manchuria had waned considerably.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_Tibet">Relations with Tibet</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_relations_during_the_Ming_dynasty" title="Sino-Tibetan relations during the Ming dynasty">Sino-Tibetan relations during the Ming dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:17th_century_Central_Tibeten_thanka_of_Guhyasamaja_Akshobhyavajra,_Rubin_Museum_of_Art.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2300" data-file-width="1776" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/17th_century_Central_Tibeten_thanka_of_Guhyasamaja_Akshobhyavajra%2C_Rubin_Museum_of_Art.jpg/220px-17th_century_Central_Tibeten_thanka_of_Guhyasamaja_Akshobhyavajra%2C_Rubin_Museum_of_Art.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/17th_century_Central_Tibeten_thanka_of_Guhyasamaja_Akshobhyavajra%2C_Rubin_Museum_of_Art.jpg/330px-17th_century_Central_Tibeten_thanka_of_Guhyasamaja_Akshobhyavajra%2C_Rubin_Museum_of_Art.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/17th_century_Central_Tibeten_thanka_of_Guhyasamaja_Akshobhyavajra%2C_Rubin_Museum_of_Art.jpg/440px-17th_century_Central_Tibeten_thanka_of_Guhyasamaja_Akshobhyavajra%2C_Rubin_Museum_of_Art.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:17th_century_Central_Tibeten_thanka_of_Guhyasamaja_Akshobhyavajra,_Rubin_Museum_of_Art.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 17th-century Tibetan <a href="/wiki/Thangka" title="Thangka">thangka</a> of Guhyasamaja Akshobhyavajra; the Ming dynasty court gathered various tribute items that were native products of Tibet (such as thangkas),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-information_office_of_the_state_council_2002_73_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-information_office_of_the_state_council_2002_73-25">[22]</a></sup> and in return granted gifts to Tibetan tribute-bearers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWangNyima199739.E2.80.9341_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWangNyima199739.E2.80.9341-26">[23]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Ming" title="History of Ming">Mingshi</a></i> – the official history of the Ming dynasty compiled by the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> in 1739 – states that the Ming established itinerant commanderies overseeing Tibetan administration while also renewing titles of ex-Yuan dynasty officials from <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a> and conferring new princely titles on leaders of <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism" title="Tibetan Buddhism">Tibetan Buddhist sects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mingshi_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-mingshi-27">[24]</a></sup> However, Turrell V. Wylie states that <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">censorship</a> in the <i>Mingshi</i> in favor of bolstering the Ming emperor's prestige and reputation at all costs obfuscates the nuanced history of Sino-Tibetan relations during the Ming era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWylie2003470_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWylie2003470-28">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Modern scholars debate whether the Ming dynasty had <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a> over Tibet. Some believe it was a relationship of loose <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a> that was largely cut off when the <a href="/wiki/Jiajing_Emperor" title="Jiajing Emperor">Jiajing Emperor</a> (r. 1521–67) persecuted Buddhism in favor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daoism" title="Daoism">Daoism</a> at court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWylie2003470_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWylie2003470-28">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWangNyima19971.E2.80.9340_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWangNyima19971.E2.80.9340-29">[26]</a></sup> Others argue that the significant religious nature of the relationship with Tibetan lamas is underrepresented in modern scholarship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorbu200152_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorbu200152-30">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKolma.C5.A1196732_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKolma.C5.A1196732-31">[28]</a></sup> Others note the Ming need for Central Asian horses and the need to maintain the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibet_during_the_Ming_dynasty#Tribute_and_exchanging_tea_for_horses" title="Tibet during the Ming dynasty">tea-horse trade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWangNyima199739.E2.80.9340_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWangNyima199739.E2.80.9340-32">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESperling2003474.E2.80.935.2C_478_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESperling2003474.E2.80.935.2C_478-33">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerdue2000273_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerdue2000273-34">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKolma.C5.A1196728.E2.80.939_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKolma.C5.A1196728.E2.80.939-35">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Ming sporadically sent armed forays into Tibet during the 14th century, which the Tibetans successfully resisted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELanglois1988139.2C_161_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELanglois1988139.2C_161-36">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeiss1988417.E2.80.938_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeiss1988417.E2.80.938-37">[34]</a></sup> Several scholars point out that unlike the preceding Mongols, the Ming dynasty did not garrison permanent troops in Tibet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999227_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999227-38">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWangNyima199738_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWangNyima199738-39">[36]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wanli_Emperor" title="Wanli Emperor">Wanli Emperor</a> (r. 1572–1620) attempted to reestablish Sino-Tibetan relations in the wake of a <a href="/wiki/History_of_Tibet#The_origin_of_the_title_of_.27Dalai_Lama.27" title="History of Tibet">Mongol-Tibetan alliance</a> initiated in 1578, an alliance which affected the foreign policy of the subsequent Manchu <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> (1644–1912) in their support for the <a href="/wiki/Dalai_Lama" title="Dalai Lama">Dalai Lama</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Gelug" title="Gelug">Yellow Hat</a> sect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWylie2003470_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWylie2003470-28">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKolma.C5.A1196730.E2.80.931_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKolma.C5.A1196730.E2.80.931-40">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldstein19978_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldstein19978-41">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ming_Biographical_Dictionary_23_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ming_Biographical_Dictionary_23-42">[39]</a></sup> By the late 16th century, the Mongols proved to be successful armed protectors of the Yellow Hat Dalai Lama after their increasing presence in the <a href="/wiki/Amdo" title="Amdo">Amdo</a> region, culminating in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_conquest_of_Tibet" title="Mongol conquest of Tibet">conquest of Tibet</a> by <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCshi_Khan" title="Güshi Khan">Güshi Khan</a> (1582–1655) in 1642,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWylie2003470_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWylie2003470-28">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKolma.C5.A1196734.E2.80.935_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKolma.C5.A1196734.E2.80.935-43">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldstein19976.E2.80.939_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldstein19976.E2.80.939-44">[41]</a></sup> establishing the <a href="/wiki/Khoshut_Khanate" title="Khoshut Khanate">Khoshut Khanate</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_of_the_Yongle_Emperor">Reign of the Yongle Emperor</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_to_power">Rise to power</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2063" data-file-width="1387" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg/170px-Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg/255px-Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg/340px-Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait of the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a> (ruled in 1402–24)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a> specified his grandson Zhu Yunwen as his successor, and he assumed the throne as the <a href="/wiki/Jianwen_Emperor" title="Jianwen Emperor">Jianwen Emperor</a> (1398–1402) after Hongwu's death in 1398. The most powerful of Hongwu's sons, Zhu Di, then the militarily mighty disagreed with this, and soon a political showdown erupted between him and his nephew Jianwen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson2000527_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson2000527-45">[42]</a></sup> After Jianwen arrested many of Zhu Di's associates, Zhu Di plotted a rebellion that sparked a <a href="/wiki/Jingnan_Campaign" title="Jingnan Campaign">three-year civil war</a>. Under the pretext of rescuing the young Jianwen from corrupting officials, Zhu Di personally led forces in the revolt; the palace in Nanjing was burned to the ground, along with Jianwen himself, his wife, mother, and courtiers. Zhu Di assumed the throne as the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a> (1402–1424); his reign is universally viewed by scholars as a "second founding" of the Ming dynasty since he reversed many of his father's policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAtwell200284_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAtwell200284-46">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_capital_and_foreign_engagement">New capital and foreign engagement</span></h4>
<p>Yongle demoted Nanjing to a secondary capital and in 1403 announced the new capital of China was to be at his power base in <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>. Construction of a new city there lasted from 1407 to 1420, employing hundreds of thousands of workers daily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006272_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006272-47">[44]</a></sup> At the center was the political node of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_City_(Beijing)" title="Imperial City (Beijing)">Imperial City</a>, and at the center of this was the <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">Forbidden City</a>, the palatial residence of the emperor and his family. By 1553, the Outer City was added to the south, which brought the overall size of Beijing to 4 by 4½ miles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999194_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999194-48">[45]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Noel_2005_P%C3%A9kin_tombeaux_Ming_voie_des_%C3%A2mes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Noel_2005_P%C3%A9kin_tombeaux_Ming_voie_des_%C3%A2mes.jpg/220px-Noel_2005_P%C3%A9kin_tombeaux_Ming_voie_des_%C3%A2mes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Noel_2005_P%C3%A9kin_tombeaux_Ming_voie_des_%C3%A2mes.jpg/330px-Noel_2005_P%C3%A9kin_tombeaux_Ming_voie_des_%C3%A2mes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Noel_2005_P%C3%A9kin_tombeaux_Ming_voie_des_%C3%A2mes.jpg/440px-Noel_2005_P%C3%A9kin_tombeaux_Ming_voie_des_%C3%A2mes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Noel_2005_P%C3%A9kin_tombeaux_Ming_voie_des_%C3%A2mes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Tombs" title="Ming Tombs">Ming Tombs</a> located 50 km (31 mi) north of <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>; the site was chosen by Yongle.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beginning in 1405, the Yongle Emperor entrusted his favored <a href="/wiki/Eunuch" title="Eunuch">eunuch</a> commander <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> (1371–1433) as the admiral for a gigantic new fleet of ships designated for international <a href="/wiki/Treasure_voyages" title="Treasure voyages">tributary missions</a>. The Chinese had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Imperial_China" title="Foreign relations of Imperial China">sent diplomatic missions</a> over land since the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> (202 BCE – 220 CE) and engaged in <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Economy of the Song dynasty">private overseas trade</a>, but these missions were unprecedented in grandeur and scale. To service seven different tributary voyages, the Nanjing shipyards constructed two thousand vessels from 1403 to 1419, including <a href="/wiki/Chinese_treasure_ship" title="Chinese treasure ship">treasure ships</a> measuring 112 m (370 ft) to 134 m (440 ft) in length and 45 m (150 ft) to 54 m (180 ft) in width.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006137_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006137-49">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Yongle also used woodblock printing to spread Chinese culture, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty_military_conquests" title="Ming dynasty military conquests">used the military</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a>) to expand China's borders. This included the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Chinese_domination_of_Vietnam" title="Fourth Chinese domination of Vietnam">brief occupation of Vietnam</a>, from the initial invasion in 1406 until the Ming withdrawal in 1427 as a result of protracted <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla warfare</a> led by the subsequent founder of the Vietnamese <a href="/wiki/L%C3%AA_dynasty" title="Lê dynasty">Lê dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang1998317.E2.80.93327_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang1998317.E2.80.93327-50">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tumu_Crisis_and_the_Ming_Mongols">Tumu Crisis and the Ming Mongols</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tumu_Crisis" title="Tumu Crisis">Tumu Crisis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rebellion_of_Cao_Qin" title="Rebellion of Cao Qin">Rebellion of Cao Qin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="759" height="336" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg/170px-Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg/255px-Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg/340px-Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bengali_people" title="Bengali people">Bengali</a> envoys presented a tribute giraffe in the name of King Saif Al-Din Hamzah Shah of Bengal (r. 1410–12) to the Yongle Emperor of Ming China (r. 1402–24).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Oirats" title="Oirats">Oirat</a> leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Esen_Tayisi" title="Esen Tayisi">Esen Tayisi</a> launched an invasion into Ming China in July 1449. The chief eunuch <a href="/wiki/Wang_Zhen_(eunuch)" title="Wang Zhen (eunuch)">Wang Zhen</a> encouraged the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhengtong_Emperor" title="Zhengtong Emperor">Zhengtong Emperor</a> (r. 1435–49) to lead a force personally to face the Oirats after a recent Ming defeat; the emperor left the capital and put his half-brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhu_Qiyu" title="Zhu Qiyu">Zhu Qiyu</a> in charge of affairs as temporary regent. On 8 September, Esen routed Zhengtong's army, and Zhengtong was captured—an event known as the <a href="/wiki/Tumu_Crisis" title="Tumu Crisis">Tumu Crisis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006273_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006273-51">[48]</a></sup> The Oirats held the Zhengtong Emperor for ransom. However, this scheme was foiled once the emperor's younger brother assumed the throne under the era name <a href="/wiki/Jingtai_Emperor" title="Jingtai Emperor">Jingtai</a> (r. 1449–57); the Oirats were also repelled once the Jingtai Emperor's confidant and defense minister <a href="/wiki/Yu_Qian" title="Yu Qian">Yu Qian</a> (1398–1457) gained control of the Ming armed forces. Holding the Zhengtong Emperor in captivity was a useless bargaining chip for the Oirats as long as another sat on his throne, so they released him back into Ming China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006273_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006273-51">[48]</a></sup> The former emperor was placed under house arrest in the palace until the coup against the Jingtai Emperor in 1457 known as the "Wresting the Gate Incident".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson199983_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson199983-52">[49]</a></sup> The former emperor retook the throne under the new era name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tianshun_Emperor" title="Tianshun Emperor">Tianshun</a> (r. 1457–64).</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tianshun_Emperor" title="Tianshun Emperor">Tianshun</a> proved to be a troubled time and Mongol forces within the Ming military structure continued to be problematic. On 7 August 1461, the Chinese general Cao Qin and his Ming troops of Mongol descent <a href="/wiki/Rebellion_of_Cao_Qin" title="Rebellion of Cao Qin">staged a coup against the Tianshun Emperor</a> out of fear of being next on his purge-list of those who aided him in the Wresting the Gate Incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson199984.E2.80.935_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson199984.E2.80.935-53">[50]</a></sup> Cao's rebel force managed to set fire to the western and eastern gates of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_City_(Beijing)" title="Imperial City (Beijing)">Imperial City</a> (doused by rain during the battle) and killed several leading ministers before his forces were finally cornered and he was forced to commit suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson199979.2C_101.E2.80.938_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson199979.2C_101.E2.80.938-54">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>While the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a> had staged <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor%27s_campaigns_against_the_Mongols" title="Yongle Emperor's campaigns against the Mongols">five major offensives north</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Wall" title="Great Wall">Great Wall</a> against the Mongols and the Oirats, the constant threat of Oirat incursions prompted the Ming authorities to fortify the Great Wall from the late 15th century to the 16th century; nevertheless, John Fairbank notes that "it proved to be a futile military gesture but vividly expressed China's siege mentality."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006139_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006139-55">[52]</a></sup> Yet the Great Wall was not meant to be a purely defensive fortification; its towers functioned rather as a series of lit beacons and signalling stations to allow rapid warning to friendly units of advancing enemy troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999208_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999208-56">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_and_fall_of_the_Ming_dynasty">Decline and fall of the Ming dynasty</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Ming_dynasty" title="Fall of the Ming dynasty">Fall of the Ming dynasty</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_of_the_Wanli_Emperor">Reign of the Wanli Emperor</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wanli-Emperor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="695" data-file-width="496" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Wanli-Emperor.jpg/170px-Wanli-Emperor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Wanli-Emperor.jpg/255px-Wanli-Emperor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Wanli-Emperor.jpg/340px-Wanli-Emperor.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wanli-Emperor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Wanli_Emperor" title="Wanli Emperor">Wanli Emperor</a> (ruled in 1572–1620) in state ceremonial court dress</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The financial drain of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imjin_War" title="Imjin War">Imjin War</a> in Korea against the Japanese was one of the many problems—fiscal or other—facing Ming China during the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Wanli_Emperor" title="Wanli Emperor">Wanli Emperor</a> (1572–1620). In the beginning of his reign, Wanli surrounded himself with able advisors and made a conscientious effort to handle state affairs. His Grand Secretary <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Juzheng" title="Zhang Juzheng">Zhang Juzheng</a> (1572–82) built up an effective network of alliances with senior officials. However, there was no one after him skilled enough to maintain the stability of these alliances;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195831_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195831-57">[54]</a></sup> officials soon banded together in opposing political factions. Over time Wanli grew tired of court affairs and frequent political quarreling amongst his ministers, preferring to stay behind the walls of the Forbidden City and out of his officials' sight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199916_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199916-58">[55]</a></sup> Scholar-officials lost prominence in administration as eunuchs became intermediaries between the aloof emperor and his officials; any senior official who wanted to discuss state matters had to persuade powerful eunuchs with a bribe simply to have his demands or message relayed to the emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199917_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199917-59">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_eunuchs">Role of eunuchs</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Porcelain_tea_cups_from_the_reign_of_the_Tianqi_Emperor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Porcelain_tea_cups_from_the_reign_of_the_Tianqi_Emperor.jpg/220px-Porcelain_tea_cups_from_the_reign_of_the_Tianqi_Emperor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Porcelain_tea_cups_from_the_reign_of_the_Tianqi_Emperor.jpg/330px-Porcelain_tea_cups_from_the_reign_of_the_Tianqi_Emperor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Porcelain_tea_cups_from_the_reign_of_the_Tianqi_Emperor.jpg/440px-Porcelain_tea_cups_from_the_reign_of_the_Tianqi_Emperor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Porcelain_tea_cups_from_the_reign_of_the_Tianqi_Emperor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Tianqi_Emperor" title="Tianqi Emperor">Tianqi-era</a> teacups, from the Nantoyōsō Collection in Japan; the Tianqi Emperor was heavily influenced and largely controlled by the eunuch <a href="/wiki/Wei_Zhongxian" title="Wei Zhongxian">Wei Zhongxian</a> (1568–1627).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Hongwu Emperor forbade eunuchs to learn how to read or engage in politics. Whether or not these restrictions were carried out with absolute success in his reign, eunuchs during the Yongle Emperor's reign and afterwards managed huge imperial workshops, commanded armies, and participated in matters of appointment and promotion of officials. The eunuchs developed their own bureaucracy that was organized parallel to but was not subject to the civil service bureaucracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999194_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999194-48">[45]</a></sup> Although there were several dictatorial eunuchs throughout the Ming, such as <a href="/wiki/Wang_Zhen_(eunuch)" title="Wang Zhen (eunuch)">Wang Zhen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wang_Zhi_(pirate)" title="Wang Zhi (pirate)">Wang Zhi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Liu_Jin" title="Liu Jin">Liu Jin</a>, excessive tyrannical eunuch power did not become evident until the 1590s when the <a href="/wiki/Wanli_Emperor" title="Wanli Emperor">Wanli Emperor</a> increased their rights over the civil bureaucracy and granted them power to collect provincial taxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199917_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199917-59">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999194.E2.80.935_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999194.E2.80.935-60">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195811_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195811-61">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>The eunuch <a href="/wiki/Wei_Zhongxian" title="Wei Zhongxian">Wei Zhongxian</a> (1568–1627) dominated the court of the <a href="/wiki/Tianqi_Emperor" title="Tianqi Emperor">Tianqi Emperor</a> (r. 1620–1627) and had his political rivals tortured to death, mostly the vocal critics from the faction of the <a href="/wiki/Donglin_movement" title="Donglin movement">Donglin Society</a>. He ordered temples built in his honor throughout the Ming Empire, and built personal palaces created with funds allocated for building the previous emperor's tombs. His friends and family gained important positions without qualifications. Wei also published a historical work lambasting and belittling his political opponents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199917.E2.80.938_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199917.E2.80.938-62">[59]</a></sup> The instability at court came right as natural calamity, pestilence, rebellion, and foreign invasion came to a peak. The <a href="/wiki/Chongzhen_Emperor" title="Chongzhen Emperor">Chongzhen Emperor</a> (r. 1627–44) had Wei dismissed from court, which led to Wei's suicide shortly after.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_breakdown_and_natural_disasters">Economic breakdown and natural disasters</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">Europeans in Medieval China</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ch%27iu_Ying_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1567" data-file-width="2024" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Ch%27iu_Ying_001.jpg/220px-Ch%27iu_Ying_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Ch%27iu_Ying_001.jpg/330px-Ch%27iu_Ying_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Ch%27iu_Ying_001.jpg/440px-Ch%27iu_Ying_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ch%27iu_Ying_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Spring morning in a Han palace</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Qiu_Ying" title="Qiu Ying">Qiu Ying</a> (1494–1552); excessive luxury and decadence marked the late Ming period, spurred by the enormous state <a href="/wiki/Bullion" title="Bullion">bullion</a> of incoming silver and by private transactions involving silver.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the last years of the Wanli era and those of his two successors, an economic crisis developed that was centered on a sudden widespread lack of the empire's chief medium of exchange: silver. <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">The Portuguese</a> first <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Perestrello" title="Rafael Perestrello">established trade with China</a> in 1516,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998124_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998124-63">[60]</a></sup> trading Japanese silver for Chinese silk,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199919.E2.80.9320_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199919.E2.80.9320-64">[61]</a></sup> and after some <a href="/wiki/Fern%C3%A3o_Pires_de_Andrade" title="Fernão Pires de Andrade">initial hostilities</a> gained consent from the Ming court in 1557 to settle <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a> as their permanent trade base in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWills1998343.E2.80.93349_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWills1998343.E2.80.93349-65">[62]</a></sup> Their role in providing silver was gradually surpassed by <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">the Spanish</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199920_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199920-66">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998205_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998205-67">[64]</a></sup> while even <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Empire" title="Dutch Empire">the Dutch</a> challenged them for control of this trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998206.2C_208_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998206.2C_208-68">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWills1998349.E2.80.93353_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWills1998349.E2.80.93353-69">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_Spain" title="Philip IV of Spain">Philip IV of Spain</a> (reigned 1621–1665) began cracking down on illegal smuggling of silver from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Spain" title="Viceroyalty of New Spain">New Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_Peru" title="Viceroyalty of Peru">Peru</a> across the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> towards China, in favor of <a href="/wiki/Manila_galleon" title="Manila galleon">shipping American-mined silver</a> through Spanish ports. In 1639 the new <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa</a> regime of Japan shut down most of its foreign trade with European powers, cutting off another source of silver coming into China. These events occurring at roughly the same time caused a dramatic spike in the value of silver and made paying taxes nearly impossible for most provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998208_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998208-70">[67]</a></sup> People began hoarding precious silver as there was progressively less of it, forcing the ratio of the value of copper to silver into a steep decline. In the 1630s a string of one thousand <a href="/wiki/Cash_(Chinese_coin)" title="Cash (Chinese coin)">copper coins</a> equaled an ounce of silver; by 1640 that sum could fetch half an ounce; and, by 1643 only one-third of an ounce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199920_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199920-66">[63]</a></sup> For peasants this meant economic disaster, since they paid taxes in silver while conducting local trade and crop sales in copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199920.E2.80.931_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199920.E2.80.931-71">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Famines became common in northern China in the early 17th century because of unusually dry and cold weather that shortened the growing season—effects of a larger ecological event now known as the <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199921_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199921-72">[69]</a></sup> Famine, alongside tax increases, widespread military desertions, a declining relief system, and natural disasters such as flooding and inability of the government to properly manage irrigation and flood-control projects caused widespread loss of life and normal civility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199921_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199921-72">[69]</a></sup> The central government, starved of resources, could do very little to mitigate the effects of these calamities. Making matters worse, a widespread epidemic spread across China from Zhejiang to Henan, killing an unknown but large number of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199922.E2.80.934_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199922.E2.80.934-73">[70]</a></sup> The deadliest earthquake of all time, the <a href="/wiki/1556_Shaanxi_earthquake" title="1556 Shaanxi earthquake">Shaanxi earthquake of 1556</a>, occurred during the <a href="/wiki/Jiajing_Emperor" title="Jiajing Emperor">Jiajing Emperor</a>'s reign, killing approximately 830,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_the_Manchu">Rise of the Manchu</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shanhaiguan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Shanhaiguan.jpg/170px-Shanhaiguan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Shanhaiguan.jpg/255px-Shanhaiguan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Shanhaiguan.jpg/340px-Shanhaiguan.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shanhaiguan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Shanhaiguan_District" title="Shanhaiguan District">Shanhaiguan</a> along the Great Wall, the gate where the Manchus were repeatedly repelled before being finally let through by <a href="/wiki/Wu_Sangui" title="Wu Sangui">Wu Sangui</a> in 1644.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_people" title="Jurchen people">Jurchen</a> tribal leader named <a href="/wiki/Nurhaci" title="Nurhaci">Nurhaci</a> (r. 1616–26), starting with just a small tribe, rapidly gained control over all the <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchurian tribes</a>. During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_invasions_of_Korea_(1592%E2%80%931598)" title="Japanese invasions of Korea (1592–1598)">Japanese invasions of Joseon Korea</a> in the 1590s, he offered to lead his tribes in support of the Ming and <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon</a> army. This offer was declined, but he was granted honorific Ming titles for his gesture. Recognizing the weakness of Ming authority north of their border, he united all of the adjacent northern tribes and consolidated power in the region surrounding his homeland as the <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jurchen Jin dynasty</a> had done previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199927_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199927-75">[72]</a></sup> In 1610, he broke relations with the Ming court, and in 1618 demanded a tribute from them to redress "Seven Grievances".</p>
<p>By 1636, Nurhaci's son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huang_Taiji" title="Huang Taiji">Huang Taiji</a> renamed his dynasty from the "Later Jin" to the "<a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Great Qing</a>" at <a href="/wiki/Shenyang" title="Shenyang">Mukden</a>, which had fallen to Qing forces in 1621 and was made their capital in 1625.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199924.2C_28_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199924.2C_28-76">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang200792_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang200792-77">[74]</a></sup> Huang Taiji also adopted the Chinese imperial title <i><a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">huangdi</a></i>, declared the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huang_Taiji" title="Huang Taiji">Chongde</a> ("Revering Virtue") era, and changed the ethnic name of his people from "<a href="/wiki/Jurchen_people" title="Jurchen people">Jurchen</a>" to "<a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Manchu</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang200792_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang200792-77">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199931_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199931-78">[75]</a></sup> In 1638 the Manchu defeated and conquered Ming China's traditional ally Joseon with an army of 100,000 troops in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Manchu_invasion_of_Korea" title="Second Manchu invasion of Korea">Second Manchu invasion of Korea</a>. Shortly after, the Koreans renounced their long-held loyalty to the Ming dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199931_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199931-78">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rebellion.2C_invasion.2C_collapse">Rebellion, invasion, collapse</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Qing_conquest_of_the_Ming" title="Qing conquest of the Ming">Qing conquest of the Ming</a></div>
<p>A peasant soldier named <a href="/wiki/Li_Zicheng" title="Li Zicheng">Li Zicheng</a> mutinied with his fellow soldiers in western Shaanxi in the early 1630s after the Ming government failed to ship much-needed supplies there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199921_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199921-72">[69]</a></sup> In 1634 he was captured by a Ming general and released only on the terms that he return to service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199921.E2.80.932_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199921.E2.80.932-79">[76]</a></sup> The agreement soon broke down when a local magistrate had thirty-six of his fellow rebels executed; Li's troops retaliated by killing the officials and continued to lead a rebellion based in Rongyang, central <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a> province by 1635.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199922_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199922-80">[77]</a></sup> By the 1640s, an ex-soldier and rival to Li—<a href="/wiki/Zhang_Xianzhong" title="Zhang Xianzhong">Zhang Xianzhong</a> (1606–47) —had created a firm rebel base in <a href="/wiki/Chengdu" title="Chengdu">Chengdu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>, while Li's center of power was in <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a> with extended influence over Shaanxi and Henan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199922_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199922-80">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1640, masses of Chinese peasants who were starving, unable to pay their taxes, and no longer in fear of the frequently defeated Chinese army, began to form into huge bands of rebels. The Chinese military, caught between fruitless efforts to defeat the Manchu raiders from the north and huge peasant revolts in the provinces, essentially fell apart. Unpaid and unfed, the army was defeated by Li Zicheng— now self-styled as the Prince of <a href="/wiki/Shun_dynasty" title="Shun dynasty">Shun</a> —and deserted the capital without much of a fight. On 26 May 1644, Beijing fell to a rebel army led by Li Zicheng when the city gates were treacherously opened from within. During the turmoil, <a href="/wiki/Chongzhen_Emperor" title="Chongzhen Emperor">the last Ming emperor</a> hanged himself <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guilty_Chinese_Scholartree" title="Guilty Chinese Scholartree">on a tree in the imperial garden</a> outside the Forbidden City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199925_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199925-81">[78]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ming_Chongzhen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="440" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ming_Chongzhen.jpg/170px-Ming_Chongzhen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ming_Chongzhen.jpg/255px-Ming_Chongzhen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ming_Chongzhen.jpg/340px-Ming_Chongzhen.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ming_Chongzhen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait of the <a href="/wiki/Chongzhen_Emperor" title="Chongzhen Emperor">Chongzhen Emperor</a> (r. 1627–1644)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Seizing opportunity, the Eight Banners crossed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Wall" title="Great Wall">Great Wall</a> after the Ming border general <a href="/wiki/Wu_Sangui" title="Wu Sangui">Wu Sangui</a> (1612–1678) opened the gates at <a href="/wiki/Shanhai_Pass" title="Shanhai Pass">Shanhai Pass</a>. This occurred shortly after he learned about the fate of the capital and an army of Li Zicheng marching towards him; weighing his options of alliance, he decided to side with the Manchus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199932.E2.80.933_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199932.E2.80.933-82">[79]</a></sup> The Eight Banners under the Manchu Prince <a href="/wiki/Dorgon" title="Dorgon">Dorgon</a> (1612–50) and Wu Sangui approached Beijing after the army sent by Li was destroyed at <a href="/wiki/Shanhaiguan_District" title="Shanhaiguan District">Shanhaiguan</a>; the Prince of Shun's army fled the capital on the fourth of June. On 6 June, the Manchus and Wu entered the capital and proclaimed the young <a href="/wiki/Shunzhi_Emperor" title="Shunzhi Emperor">Shunzhi Emperor</a> ruler of China. After being forced out of <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a> by the Qing, chased along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_River_(Hanshui)" title="Han River (Hanshui)">Han River</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wuchang,_Hubei" title="Wuchang, Hubei">Wuchang</a>, and finally along the northern border of <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a> province, Li Zicheng died there in the summer of 1645, thus ending the <a href="/wiki/Shun_dynasty" title="Shun dynasty">Shun dynasty</a>. One report says his death was a suicide; another states that he was beaten to death by peasants after he was caught stealing their food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199933_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199933-83">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the loss of Beijing and the death of the emperor, the Ming were not yet totally destroyed. Nanjing, Fujian, Guangdong, Shanxi, and Yunnan were all strongholds of Ming resistance. However, there were several pretenders for the Ming throne, and their forces were divided. These scattered Ming remnants in southern China after 1644 were collectively designated by 19th-century historians as the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ming" title="Southern Ming">Southern Ming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> Each bastion of resistance was individually defeated by the Qing until 1662, when the last southern Ming Emperor died, the Yongli Emperor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prince_of_Gui" title="Prince of Gui">Zhu Youlang</a>. The last Ming Princes to hold out were the Prince of Ningjing <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Shugui" title="Zhu Shugui">Zhu Shugui</a> and the son of <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Yihai" title="Zhu Yihai">Zhu Yihai</a>, the Prince of Lu <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Zhu_Honghuan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zhu Honghuan (page does not exist)">Zhu Honghuan</a> (朱弘桓) who stayed with <a href="/wiki/Koxinga" title="Koxinga">Koxinga</a>'s Ming loyalists in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Tungning" title="Kingdom of Tungning">Kingdom of Tungning</a> until 1683. Zhu Shugui proclaimed that he acted in the name of the deceased <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yongli_Emperor" title="Yongli Emperor">Yongli Emperor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shepherd1993_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shepherd1993-85">[82]</a></sup> The Qing eventually sent the seventeen Ming princes still living in Taiwan back to mainland China where they spent the rest of their lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1725 the Qing <a href="/wiki/Yongzheng_Emperor" title="Yongzheng Emperor">Yongzheng Emperor</a> bestowed the hereditary title of Marquis on a descendant of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Zhu" title="House of Zhu">Ming dynasty Imperial family</a>, Zhu Zhilian (朱之璉), who received a salary from the Qing government and whose duty was to perform rituals at the <a href="/wiki/Ming_tombs" title="Ming tombs">Ming tombs</a>, and was also inducted the Chinese Plain White Banner in the <a href="/wiki/Eight_Banners" title="Eight Banners">Eight Banners</a>. Later the <a href="/wiki/Qianlong_Emperor" title="Qianlong Emperor">Qianlong Emperor</a> bestowed the title <a href="/wiki/Marquis_of_Extended_Grace" title="Marquis of Extended Grace">Marquis of Extended Grace</a> posthumously on Zhu Zhilian in 1750, and the title passed on through twelve generations of Ming descendants until the end of the Qing dynasty in 1912. The last Marquis of Extended Grance was Zhu Yuxun (朱煜勳). In 1912, after the overthrow of the Qing dynasty in the <a href="/wiki/Xinhai_Revolution" title="Xinhai Revolution">Xinhai Revolution</a>, some advocated that a Han Chinese be installed as Emperor, either the descendant of Confucius, who was the <a href="/wiki/Duke_Yansheng" title="Duke Yansheng">Duke Yansheng</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodhouse2004_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woodhouse2004-87">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spence1982_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spence1982-88">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuLiu1983_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-HuLiu1983-89">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup> or the Ming dynasty Imperial family descendant, the <a href="/wiki/Marquis_of_Extended_Grace" title="Marquis of Extended Grace">Marquis of Extended Grace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kent1912_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kent1912-92">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aldrich2008_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aldrich2008-93">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Province.2C_prefecture.2C_subprefecture.2C_county">Province, prefecture, subprefecture, county</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PanYongzheng-ProcessionalTombFigurines-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1680" data-file-width="3600" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/PanYongzheng-ProcessionalTombFigurines-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg/220px-PanYongzheng-ProcessionalTombFigurines-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/PanYongzheng-ProcessionalTombFigurines-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg/330px-PanYongzheng-ProcessionalTombFigurines-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/PanYongzheng-ProcessionalTombFigurines-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg/440px-PanYongzheng-ProcessionalTombFigurines-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PanYongzheng-ProcessionalTombFigurines-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Processional figurines from the <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a> tomb of Pan Yongzheng, a Ming dynasty official who lived during the 16th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Ming emperors took over the provincial administration system of the Yuan dynasty, and the thirteen Ming provinces are the precursors of the modern provinces. Throughout the Song dynasty, the largest political division was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circuit_(country_subdivision)" title="Circuit (country subdivision)">circuit</a> (<i>lu</i> 路).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYuan1994193.E2.80.934_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYuan1994193.E2.80.934-94">[91]</a></sup> However, after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jingkang_Incident" title="Jingkang Incident">Jurchen invasion</a> in 1127, the Song court established four semi-autonomous regional command systems based on territorial and military units, with a detached service secretariat that would become the provincial administrations of the Yuan, Ming, and Qing dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwell1982397.E2.80.938_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwell1982397.E2.80.938-95">[92]</a></sup> Copied on the Yuan model, the Ming provincial bureaucracy contained three commissions: one civil, one military, and one for surveillance. Below the level of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Province_(China)" title="Province (China)">province</a> (<i>sheng</i> 省) were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prefecture_(China)" title="Prefecture (China)">prefectures</a> (<i>fu</i> 府) operating under a prefect (<i>zhifu</i> 知府), followed by <a href="/wiki/Zhou_(country_subdivision)" title="Zhou (country subdivision)">subprefectures</a> (<i>zhou</i> 州) under a subprefect. The lowest unit was the <a href="/wiki/Counties_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China#History" title="Counties of the People's Republic of China">county</a> (<i>xian</i> 縣), overseen by a magistrate. Besides the provinces, there were also two large areas that belonged to no province, but were metropolitan areas (<i>jing</i> 亰) attached to Nanjing and Beijing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker19585_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker19585-96">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutions_and_bureaus">Institutions and bureaus</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutional_trends">Institutional trends</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gugong.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Gugong.jpg/220px-Gugong.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Gugong.jpg/330px-Gugong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Gugong.jpg/440px-Gugong.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gugong.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">Forbidden City</a>, the official imperial household of the Ming and Qing dynasties from 1420 until 1924, when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a> evicted <a href="/wiki/Puyi" title="Puyi">Puyi</a> from the Inner Court.</div>
</div>
</div>
