<p>Departing from the main central administrative system generally known as the <a href="/wiki/Three_Departments_and_Six_Ministries" title="Three Departments and Six Ministries">Three Departments and Six Ministries</a> system, <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Government of the Han dynasty">which was instituted by various dynasties</a> since late <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a> (202 BCE – 220 CE), the Ming administration had only one Department, the Secretariat, that controlled the Six Ministries. Following the execution of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chancellor_(China)" title="Chancellor (China)">Chancellor</a> Hu Weiyong in 1380, the Hongwu Emperor abolished the Secretariat, the <a href="/wiki/Censorate" title="Censorate">Censorate</a>, and the Chief Military Commission and personally took charge of the Six Ministries and the regional Five Military Commissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195828_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195828-97">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang2007p._15.2C_footnote_42_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang2007p._15.2C_footnote_42-98">[95]</a></sup> Thus a whole level of administration was cut out and only partially rebuilt by subsequent rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195828_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195828-97">[94]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Secretariat" title="Grand Secretariat">Grand Secretariat</a>, at the beginning a secretarial institution that assisted the emperor with administrative paperwork, was instituted, but without employing grand counselors, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chancellor_(China)" title="Chancellor (China)">chancellors</a>.</p>
<p>The Hongwu Emperor sent his heir apparent to Shaanxi in 1391 to "tour and soothe" (<i>xunfu</i>) the region; in 1421 the Yongle Emperor commissioned 26 officials to travel the empire and uphold similar investigatory and patrimonial duties. By 1430 these <i>xunfu</i> assignments became institutionalized as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_coordinator" title="Grand coordinator">grand coordinators</a>". Hence, the Censorate was reinstalled and first staffed with investigating censors, later with censors-in-chief. By 1453, the grand coordinators were granted the title vice censor-in-chief or assistant censor-in-chief and were allowed direct access to the emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang200716_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang200716-99">[96]</a></sup> As in prior dynasties, the provincial administrations were monitored by a travelling inspector from the Censorate. Censors had the power to impeach officials on an irregular basis, unlike the senior officials who were to do so only in triennial evaluations of junior officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang200716_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang200716-99">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195816_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195816-100">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although decentralization of state power within the provinces occurred in the early Ming, the trend of central government officials delegated to the provinces as virtual provincial governors began in the 1420s. By the late Ming dynasty, there were central government officials delegated to two or more provinces as supreme commanders and viceroys, a system which reined in the power and influence of the military by the civil establishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195823_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195823-101">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Grand_Secretariat_and_Six_Ministries">Grand Secretariat and Six Ministries</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Jiang_Shunfu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="452" data-file-width="450" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Portrait_of_Jiang_Shunfu.jpg/170px-Portrait_of_Jiang_Shunfu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Portrait_of_Jiang_Shunfu.jpg/255px-Portrait_of_Jiang_Shunfu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Portrait_of_Jiang_Shunfu.jpg/340px-Portrait_of_Jiang_Shunfu.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Jiang_Shunfu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A portrait of <a href="/wiki/Jiang_Shunfu" title="Jiang Shunfu">Jiang Shunfu</a>, an official under the <a href="/wiki/Hongzhi_Emperor" title="Hongzhi Emperor">Hongzhi Emperor</a>, now in the <a href="/wiki/Nanjing_Museum" title="Nanjing Museum">Nanjing Museum</a>. The decoration of two <a href="/wiki/Crane_(bird)" title="Crane (bird)">cranes</a> on his chest is a "<a href="/wiki/Mandarin_square" title="Mandarin square">rank badge</a>" that indicates he was a civil official of the first rank.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Governmental institutions in China conformed to a similar pattern for some two thousand years, but each dynasty installed special offices and bureaus, reflecting its own particular interests. The Ming administration utilized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Secretaries" title="Grand Secretaries">Grand Secretaries</a> to assist the emperor, handling paperwork under the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a> and later appointed as top officials of agencies and Grand Preceptor, a top-ranking, non-functional civil service post, under the <a href="/wiki/Hongxi_Emperor" title="Hongxi Emperor">Hongxi Emperor</a> (ruled 1424–25).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195829.E2.80.9330_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195829.E2.80.9330-102">[99]</a></sup> The Grand Secretariat drew its members from the <a href="/wiki/Hanlin_Academy" title="Hanlin Academy">Hanlin Academy</a> and were considered part of the imperial authority, not the ministerial one (hence being at odds with both the emperor and ministers at times).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195830_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195830-103">[100]</a></sup> The Secretariat operated as a coordinating agency, whereas the Six Ministries – Personnel, Revenue, Rites, War, Justice, and Public Works – were direct administrative organs of the state:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195831.E2.80.9332_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195831.E2.80.9332-104">[101]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>The Ministry of Personnel was in charge of appointments, merit ratings, promotions, and demotions of officials, as well as granting of honorific titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195832_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195832-105">[102]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Ministry of Revenue was in charge of gathering census data, collecting taxes, and handling state revenues, while there were two offices of currency that were subordinate to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195833_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195833-106">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Ministry of Rites was in charge of state ceremonies, rituals, and sacrifices; it also oversaw registers for Buddhist and Daoist priesthoods and even the reception of envoys from tributary states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195833.E2.80.9335_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195833.E2.80.9335-107">[104]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Ministry of War was in charge of the appointments, promotions, and demotions of military officers, the maintenance of military installations, equipment, and weapons, as well as the courier system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195835_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195835-108">[105]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Ministry of Justice was in charge of judicial and penal processes, but had no supervisory role over the Censorate or the Grand Court of Revision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195836_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195836-109">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Ministry of Works had charge of government construction projects, hiring of artisans and laborers for temporary service, manufacturing government equipment, the maintenance of roads and canals, standardization of weights and measures, and the gathering of resources from the countryside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195836_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195836-109">[106]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bureaus_and_offices_for_the_imperial_household">Bureaus and offices for the imperial household</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ming_coinage_14th_17th_century.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="519" data-file-width="1459" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Ming_coinage_14th_17th_century.jpg/220px-Ming_coinage_14th_17th_century.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Ming_coinage_14th_17th_century.jpg/330px-Ming_coinage_14th_17th_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Ming_coinage_14th_17th_century.jpg/440px-Ming_coinage_14th_17th_century.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ming_coinage_14th_17th_century.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_coinage_in_the_Ming_dynasty" title="Chinese coinage in the Ming dynasty">Ming coinage</a>, 14–17th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The imperial household was staffed almost entirely by eunuchs and ladies with their own bureaus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195824_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195824-110">[107]</a></sup> Female servants were organized into the Bureau of Palace Attendance, Bureau of Ceremonies, Bureau of Apparel, Bureau of Foodstuffs, Bureau of the Bedchamber, Bureau of Handicrafts, and Office of Staff Surveillance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195824_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195824-110">[107]</a></sup> Starting in the 1420s, eunuchs began taking over these ladies' positions until only the Bureau of Apparel with its four subsidiary offices remained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195824_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195824-110">[107]</a></sup> Hongwu had his eunuchs organized into the Directorate of Palace Attendants, but as eunuch power at court increased, so did their administrative offices, with eventual twelve directorates, four offices, and eight bureaus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195824_110-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195824-110">[107]</a></sup> The dynasty had a vast imperial household, staffed with thousands of eunuchs, who were headed by the Directorate of Palace Attendants. The eunuchs were divided into different directorates in charge of staff surveillance, ceremonial rites, food, utensils, documents, stables, seals, apparel, and so on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195825_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195825-111">[108]</a></sup> The offices were in charge of providing fuel, music, paper, and baths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195825_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195825-111">[108]</a></sup> The bureaus were in charge of weapons, silverwork, laundering, headgear, bronzework, textile manufacture, wineries, and gardens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195825_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195825-111">[108]</a></sup> At times, the most influential eunuch in the Directorate of Ceremonial acted as a <i>de facto</i> dictator over the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195811.2C_25_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195811.2C_25-112">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the imperial household was staffed mostly by eunuchs and palace ladies, there was a civil service office called the Seal Office, which cooperated with eunuch agencies in maintaining imperial seals, tallies, and stamps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195825.E2.80.9326_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195825.E2.80.9326-113">[110]</a></sup> There were also civil service offices to oversee the affairs of imperial princes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195826_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195826-114">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel">Personnel</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholar-officials">Scholar-officials</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ming-Beamtenpr%C3%BCfungen1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="688" data-file-width="591" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Ming-Beamtenpr%C3%BCfungen1.jpg/170px-Ming-Beamtenpr%C3%BCfungen1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Ming-Beamtenpr%C3%BCfungen1.jpg/255px-Ming-Beamtenpr%C3%BCfungen1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Ming-Beamtenpr%C3%BCfungen1.jpg/340px-Ming-Beamtenpr%C3%BCfungen1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ming-Beamtenpr%C3%BCfungen1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Candidates who had taken the civil service examinations would crowd around the wall where the results were posted; detail from a handscroll in ink and color on silk, by <a href="/wiki/Qiu_Ying" title="Qiu Ying">Qiu Ying</a> (1494–1552).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999200_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999200-115">[112]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Hongwu emperor from 1373 to 1384 staffed his bureaus with officials gathered through recommendations only. After that the scholar-officials who populated the many ranks of bureaucracy were recruited through a rigorous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_examinations" title="Imperial examinations">examination system</a> that was initially established by the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a> (581–618).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195812_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195812-116">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200696_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais200696-117">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999145.E2.80.936_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999145.E2.80.936-118">[115]</a></sup> Theoretically the system of exams allowed anyone to join the ranks of imperial officials (although it was frowned upon for merchants to join); in reality the time and funding needed to support the study in preparation for the exam generally limited participants to those already coming from the landholding class. However, the government did exact provincial quotas while drafting officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999199_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999199-119">[116]</a></sup> This was an effort to curb monopolization of power by landholding gentry who came from the most prosperous regions, where education was the most advanced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999198.E2.80.939_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999198.E2.80.939-120">[117]</a></sup> The expansion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technology_of_the_Song_dynasty#Movable_type_printing" title="Technology of the Song dynasty">printing industry since Song times</a> enhanced the spread of knowledge and number of potential exam candidates throughout the provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999201.E2.80.932_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999201.E2.80.932-121">[118]</a></sup> For young schoolchildren there were printed <a href="/wiki/Multiplication_table" title="Multiplication table">multiplication tables</a> and <a href="/wiki/Primer_(textbook)" title="Primer (textbook)">primers</a> for elementary vocabulary; for adult examination candidates there were mass-produced, inexpensive volumes of Confucian classics and successful examination answers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999202_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999202-122">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>As in earlier periods, the focus of the examination was classical Confucian texts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195812_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195812-116">[113]</a></sup> while the bulk of test material centered on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_Books" title="Four Books">Four Books</a> outlined by <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> in the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999198_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999198-123">[120]</a></sup> Ming era examinations were perhaps more difficult to pass since the 1487 requirement of completing the "<a href="/wiki/Eight-legged_essay" title="Eight-legged essay">eight-legged essay</a>", a departure from basing essays off progressing literary trends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999198_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999198-123">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195813_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195813-124">[121]</a></sup> The exams increased in difficulty as the student progressed from the local level, and appropriate titles were accordingly awarded successful applicants. Officials were classified in nine hierarchic grades, each grade divided into two degrees, with ranging salaries (nominally paid in piculs of rice) according to their rank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195811.E2.80.932_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195811.E2.80.932-125">[122]</a></sup> While provincial graduates who were appointed to office were immediately assigned to low-ranking posts like the county graduates, those who passed the palace examination were awarded a <i>jinshi</i> ('presented scholar') degree and assured a high-level position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195814_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195814-126">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998xxv_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998xxv-127">[124]</a></sup> In 276 years of Ming rule and ninety palace examinations, the number of doctoral degrees granted by passing the palace examinations was 24,874.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195814_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195814-126">[123]</a></sup> Ebrey states that "there were only two to four thousand of these <i>jinshi</i> at any given time, on the order of one out of 10,000 adult males."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999199_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999199-119">[116]</a></sup> This was in comparison to the 100,000 <i>shengyuan</i> ('government students'), the lowest tier of graduates, by the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999199_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999199-119">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>The maximum tenure in office was nine years, but every three years officials were graded on their performance by senior officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195815.E2.80.936_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195815.E2.80.936-128">[125]</a></sup> If they were graded as superior then they were promoted, if graded adequate then they retained their ranks, and if graded inadequate they were demoted one rank. In extreme cases, officials would be dismissed or punished. Only capital officials of grade 4 and above were exempt from the scrutiny of recorded evaluation, although they were expected to confess any of their faults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195816_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195816-100">[97]</a></sup> There were over 4,000 school instructors in county and prefectural schools who were subject to evaluations every nine years. The Chief Instructor on the prefectural level was classified as equal to a second-grade county graduate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195817_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195817-129">[126]</a></sup> The Supervisorate of Imperial Instruction oversaw the education of the heir apparent to the throne; this office was headed by a Grand Supervisor of Instruction, who was ranked as first class of grade three.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195826_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195826-114">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lesser_functionaries">Lesser functionaries</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ming_Emperor_Xuande_playing_Golf.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="367" data-file-width="749" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Ming_Emperor_Xuande_playing_Golf.jpg/220px-Ming_Emperor_Xuande_playing_Golf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Ming_Emperor_Xuande_playing_Golf.jpg/330px-Ming_Emperor_Xuande_playing_Golf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Ming_Emperor_Xuande_playing_Golf.jpg/440px-Ming_Emperor_Xuande_playing_Golf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ming_Emperor_Xuande_playing_Golf.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Xuande_Emperor" title="Xuande Emperor">Xuande Emperor</a> playing <a href="/wiki/Chuiwan" title="Chuiwan">chuiwan</a> with his eunuchs, a game similar to <a href="/wiki/Golf" title="Golf">golf</a>, by an anonymous court painter of the Xuande period (1425–35).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Scholar-officials who entered civil service through examinations acted as executive officials to a much larger body of non-ranked personnel called lesser functionaries. They outnumbered officials by four to one; Charles Hucker estimates that they were perhaps as many as 100,000 throughout the empire. These lesser functionaries performed clerical and technical tasks for government agencies. Yet they should not be confused with lowly lictors, runners, and bearers; lesser functionaries were given periodic merit evaluations like officials and after nine years of service might be accepted into a low civil service rank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195818_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195818-130">[127]</a></sup> The one great advantage of the lesser functionaries over officials was that officials were periodically rotated and assigned to different regional posts and had to rely on the good service and cooperation of the local lesser functionaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195818.E2.80.939_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195818.E2.80.939-131">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eunuchs.2C_princes.2C_and_generals">Eunuchs, princes, and generals</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_The_Emperor%27s_Approach,_Xuande_period.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4431" data-file-width="4451" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Detail_of_The_Emperor%27s_Approach%2C_Xuande_period.jpg/220px-Detail_of_The_Emperor%27s_Approach%2C_Xuande_period.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Detail_of_The_Emperor%27s_Approach%2C_Xuande_period.jpg/330px-Detail_of_The_Emperor%27s_Approach%2C_Xuande_period.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Detail_of_The_Emperor%27s_Approach%2C_Xuande_period.jpg/440px-Detail_of_The_Emperor%27s_Approach%2C_Xuande_period.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_The_Emperor%27s_Approach,_Xuande_period.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Detail of <i>The Emperor's Approach</i> showing the <a href="/wiki/Wanli_Emperor" title="Wanli Emperor">Wanli Emperor</a>'s royal carriage being pulled by elephants and escorted by cavalry (<a href="/wiki/File:Departure_Herald-Ming_Dynasty.jpg" title="File:Departure Herald-Ming Dynasty.jpg">full panoramic painting here</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Eunuchs gained unprecedented power over state affairs during the Ming dynasty. One of the most effective means of control was the secret service stationed in what was called the Eastern Depot at the beginning of the dynasty, later the Western Depot. This secret service was overseen by the Directorate of Ceremonial, hence this state organ's often totalitarian affiliation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195825_111-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195825-111">[108]</a></sup> Eunuchs had ranks that were equivalent to civil service ranks, only theirs had four grades instead of nine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195824.E2.80.935_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195824.E2.80.935-132">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Descendants of the first Ming emperor were made princes and given (typically nominal) military commands, annual stipends, and large estates. The title used was "king" (<span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">王</span>, <i>wáng</i>) but – unlike the princes in the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" title="Jin dynasty (265–420)">Jin</a> dynasties – these estates were not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feudatories" title="Feudatories">feudatories</a>, the princes did not serve any administrative function, and they partook in military affairs only during the reigns of the first two emperors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker19588_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker19588-133">[130]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Jingnan_Campaign" title="Jingnan Campaign">rebellion of the Prince of Yan</a> was justified in part as upholding the rights of the princes, but once the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a> was enthroned, he continued his nephew's policy of disarming his brothers and moved their fiefs away from the militarized northern border. Although princes served no organ of state administration, the princes, consorts of the imperial princesses, and ennobled relatives did staff the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Clan_Court" title="Imperial Clan Court">Imperial Clan Court</a>, which supervised the imperial genealogy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195826_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195826-114">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like scholar-officials, military generals were ranked in a hierarchic grading system and were given merit evaluations every five years (as opposed to three years for officials).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195819_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195819-134">[131]</a></sup> However, military officers had less prestige than officials. This was due to their hereditary service (instead of solely merit-based) and Confucian values that dictated those who chose the profession of violence (wu) over the cultured pursuits of knowledge (wen).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195819_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195819-134">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006109.E2.80.9312_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006109.E2.80.9312-135">[132]</a></sup> Although seen as less prestigious, military officers were not excluded from taking civil service examinations, and after 1478 the military even held their own examinations to test military skills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHucker195819.E2.80.9320_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHucker195819.E2.80.9320-136">[133]</a></sup> In addition to taking over the established bureaucratic structure from the Yuan period, the Ming emperors established the new post of the travelling military inspector. In the early half of the dynasty, men of noble lineage dominated the higher ranks of military office; this trend was reversed during the latter half of the dynasty as men from more humble origins eventually displaced them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson1999116.E2.80.937_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson1999116.E2.80.937-137">[134]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_arts">Literature and arts</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty_painting" title="Ming dynasty painting">Ming dynasty painting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ming_poetry" title="Ming poetry">Ming poetry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lofty_Mt.Lu_by_Shen_Zhou.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3549" data-file-width="1799" height="434" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Lofty_Mt.Lu_by_Shen_Zhou.jpg/220px-Lofty_Mt.Lu_by_Shen_Zhou.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Lofty_Mt.Lu_by_Shen_Zhou.jpg/330px-Lofty_Mt.Lu_by_Shen_Zhou.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Lofty_Mt.Lu_by_Shen_Zhou.jpg/440px-Lofty_Mt.Lu_by_Shen_Zhou.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lofty_Mt.Lu_by_Shen_Zhou.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Lofty <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lu" title="Mount Lu">Mount Lu</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Shen_Zhou" title="Shen Zhou">Shen Zhou</a>, 1467.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chinese_literature" title="Chinese literature">Literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">painting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_poetry" title="Chinese poetry">poetry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_of_China" title="Music of China">music</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_opera" title="Chinese opera">Chinese opera</a> of various types flourished during the Ming dynasty, especially in the economically prosperous lower Yangzi valley.</p>
<p>Although short fiction had been popular as far back as the Tang dynasty (618–907),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006104.E2.80.935_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006104.E2.80.935-138">[135]</a></sup> and the works of contemporaneous authors such as Xu Guangqi, Xu Xiake, and Song Yingxing were often technical and encyclopedic, the most striking literary development was the vernacular novel. While the gentry elite were educated enough to fully comprehend the language of <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a>, those with rudimentary education – such as women in educated families, merchants, and shop clerks – became a large potential audience for literature and performing arts that employed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vernacular_Chinese" title="Vernacular Chinese">Vernacular Chinese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999202.E2.80.933_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999202.E2.80.933-139">[136]</a></sup> Literati scholars edited or developed <a href="/wiki/Four_Great_Classical_Novels" title="Four Great Classical Novels">major Chinese novels</a> into mature form in this period, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Water_Margin" title="Water Margin">Water Margin</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_West" title="Journey to the West">Journey to the West</a></i>. <i><a href="/wiki/Jin_Ping_Mei" title="Jin Ping Mei">Jin Ping Mei</a></i>, published in 1610, although incorporating earlier material, marks the trend toward independent composition and concern with psychology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup> In the later years of the dynasty, <a href="/wiki/Feng_Menglong" title="Feng Menglong">Feng Menglong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ling_Mengchu" title="Ling Mengchu">Ling Mengchu</a> innovated with vernacular short fiction. Theater scripts were equally imaginative. The most famous, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Peony_Pavilion" title="The Peony Pavilion">The Peony Pavilion</a></i>, was written by <a href="/wiki/Tang_Xianzu" title="Tang Xianzu">Tang Xianzu</a> (1550–1616), with its first performance at the <a href="/wiki/Pavilion_of_Prince_Teng" title="Pavilion of Prince Teng">Pavilion of Prince Teng</a> in 1598.</p>
<p>Informal essay and travel writing was another highlight. <a href="/wiki/Xu_Xiake" title="Xu Xiake">Xu Xiake</a> (1587–1641), a <a href="/wiki/Travel_literature" title="Travel literature">travel literature</a> author, published his <i>Travel Diaries</i> in 404,000 written <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">characters</a>, with information on everything from local <a href="/wiki/History_of_geography" title="History of geography">geography</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mineralogy" title="Mineralogy">mineralogy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1959524_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1959524-141">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHargett198569_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHargett198569-142">[139]</a></sup> The first reference to the publishing of private newspapers in Beijing was in 1582; by 1638 the Beijing <a href="/wiki/Gazette" title="Gazette">Gazette</a> switched from using <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">woodblock print</a> to <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> printing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998xxi_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998xxi-143">[140]</a></sup> The new literary field of the moral guide to business ethics was developed during the late Ming period, for the readership of the merchant class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998215.E2.80.937_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998215.E2.80.937-144">[141]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mingdynasty.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="322" data-file-width="190" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bb/Mingdynasty.jpg" width="190"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mingdynasty.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Poetry of Min Ding, 17th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In contrast to Xu Xiake, who focused on technical aspects in his travel literature, the Chinese poet and official <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Hongdao" title="Yuan Hongdao">Yuan Hongdao</a> (1568–1610) used travel literature to express his desires for individualism as well as autonomy from and frustration with Confucian court politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang2007318.E2.80.939_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang2007318.E2.80.939-145">[142]</a></sup> Yuan desired to free himself from the ethical compromises that were inseparable from the career of a scholar-official. This anti-official sentiment in Yuan's travel literature and poetry was actually following in the tradition of the Song dynasty poet and official <a href="/wiki/Su_Shi" title="Su Shi">Su Shi</a> (1037–1101).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang2007319_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang2007319-146">[143]</a></sup> Yuan Hongdao and his two brothers, Yuan Zongdao (1560–1600) and <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Zhongdao" title="Yuan Zhongdao">Yuan Zhongdao</a> (1570–1623), were the founders of the Gong'an School of letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang2007318_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang2007318-147">[144]</a></sup> This highly individualistic school of poetry and prose was criticized by the Confucian establishment for its association with intense sensual lyricism, which was also apparent in Ming vernacular novels such as the <i>Jin Ping Mei</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang2007318_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang2007318-147">[144]</a></sup> Yet even gentry and scholar-officials were affected by the new popular romantic literature, seeking courtesans as soulmates to reenact the heroic love stories that arranged marriages often could not provide or accommodate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998229.E2.80.9331_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998229.E2.80.9331-148">[145]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chen_Hongshou,_leaf_album_painting.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1948" data-file-width="1820" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Chen_Hongshou%2C_leaf_album_painting.jpg/220px-Chen_Hongshou%2C_leaf_album_painting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Chen_Hongshou%2C_leaf_album_painting.jpg/330px-Chen_Hongshou%2C_leaf_album_painting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Chen_Hongshou%2C_leaf_album_painting.jpg/440px-Chen_Hongshou%2C_leaf_album_painting.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chen_Hongshou,_leaf_album_painting.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Painting of flowers, a butterfly, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_scholar%27s_rocks" title="Chinese scholar's rocks">rock sculpture</a> by <a href="/wiki/Chen_Hongshou" title="Chen Hongshou">Chen Hongshou</a> (1598–1652); small leaf album paintings like this one first <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Culture of the Song dynasty">became popular in the Song dynasty</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Famous painters included <a href="/wiki/Ni_Zan" title="Ni Zan">Ni Zan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dong_Qichang" title="Dong Qichang">Dong Qichang</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Four_Masters_of_the_Ming_dynasty" title="Four Masters of the Ming dynasty">Four Masters of the Ming dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shen_Zhou" title="Shen Zhou">Shen Zhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tang_Yin" title="Tang Yin">Tang Yin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wen_Zhengming" title="Wen Zhengming">Wen Zhengming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Qiu_Ying" title="Qiu Ying">Qiu Ying</a>. They drew upon the techniques, styles, and complexity in painting achieved by their Song and Yuan predecessors, but added techniques and styles. Well-known Ming artists could make a living simply by painting due to the high prices they demanded for their artworks and the great demand by the highly cultured community to collect precious works of art. The artist Qiu Ying was once paid 2.8 kg (100 oz) of silver to paint a long handscroll for the eightieth birthday celebration of the mother of a wealthy patron. Renowned artists often gathered an entourage of followers, some who were amateurs who painted while pursuing an official career and others who were full-time painters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999201_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999201-149">[146]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ming_dynasty_Xuande_mark_and_period_(1426%E2%80%9335)_imperial_blue_and_white_vase,_from_The_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art._%E6%98%8E%E5%AE%A3%E5%BE%B7_%E6%99%AF%E5%BE%B7%E9%8E%AE%E7%AA%AF%E9%9D%92%E8%8A%B1%E8%B2%AB%E8%80%B3%E7%93%B6,_%E7%BA%BD%E7%BA%A6%E5%A4%A7%E9%83%BD%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A6%86_.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2565" data-file-width="1297" height="435" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Ming_dynasty_Xuande_mark_and_period_%281426%E2%80%9335%29_imperial_blue_and_white_vase%2C_from_The_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art._%E6%98%8E%E5%AE%A3%E5%BE%B7_%E6%99%AF%E5%BE%B7%E9%8E%AE%E7%AA%AF%E9%9D%92%E8%8A%B1%E8%B2%AB%E8%80%B3%E7%93%B6%2C_%E7%BA%BD%E7%BA%A6%E5%A4%A7%E9%83%BD%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A6%86_.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Ming_dynasty_Xuande_mark_and_period_%281426%E2%80%9335%29_imperial_blue_and_white_vase%2C_from_The_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art._%E6%98%8E%E5%AE%A3%E5%BE%B7_%E6%99%AF%E5%BE%B7%E9%8E%AE%E7%AA%AF%E9%9D%92%E8%8A%B1%E8%B2%AB%E8%80%B3%E7%93%B6%2C_%E7%BA%BD%E7%BA%A6%E5%A4%A7%E9%83%BD%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A6%86_.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Ming_dynasty_Xuande_mark_and_period_%281426%E2%80%9335%29_imperial_blue_and_white_vase%2C_from_The_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art._%E6%98%8E%E5%AE%A3%E5%BE%B7_%E6%99%AF%E5%BE%B7%E9%8E%AE%E7%AA%AF%E9%9D%92%E8%8A%B1%E8%B2%AB%E8%80%B3%E7%93%B6%2C_%E7%BA%BD%E7%BA%A6%E5%A4%A7%E9%83%BD%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A6%86_.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ming_dynasty_Xuande_mark_and_period_(1426%E2%80%9335)_imperial_blue_and_white_vase,_from_The_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art._%E6%98%8E%E5%AE%A3%E5%BE%B7_%E6%99%AF%E5%BE%B7%E9%8E%AE%E7%AA%AF%E9%9D%92%E8%8A%B1%E8%B2%AB%E8%80%B3%E7%93%B6,_%E7%BA%BD%E7%BA%A6%E5%A4%A7%E9%83%BD%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A6%86_.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ming dynasty <a href="/wiki/Xuande_Emperor" title="Xuande Emperor">Xuande</a> mark and period (1426–35) imperial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_and_white_porcelain" title="Blue and white porcelain">blue and white</a> vase. <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a>, New York.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The period was also renowned for ceramics and porcelains.The major production centers for porcelain were the imperial kilns at <a href="/wiki/Jingdezhen" title="Jingdezhen">Jingdezhen</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a> province and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dehua" title="Dehua">Dehua</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> province. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dehua_porcelain_factories" title="Dehua porcelain factories">Dehua porcelain factories</a> catered to European tastes by creating <a href="/wiki/Chinese_export_porcelain" title="Chinese export porcelain">Chinese export porcelain</a> by the 16th century. Individual potters also became known, such as <a href="/wiki/He_Chaozong" title="He Chaozong">He Chaozong</a>, who became famous in the early 17th century for his style of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blanc-de-Chine" title="Blanc-de-Chine">white porcelain</a> sculpture. In <i>The Ceramic Trade in Asia</i>, Chuimei Ho estimates that about 16% of late Ming era Chinese ceramic exports were sent to Europe, while the rest were destined for Japan and South East Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998206_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998206-150">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carved_lacquer" title="Carved lacquer">Carved designs in lacquerware</a> and designs glazed onto <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a> wares displayed intricate scenes similar in complexity to those in painting. These items could be found in the homes of the wealthy, alongside embroidered silks and wares in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_jade" title="Chinese jade">jade</a>, ivory, and <a href="/wiki/Cloisonn%C3%A9" title="Cloisonné">cloisonné</a>. The houses of the rich were also furnished with rosewood furniture and feathery <a href="/wiki/Latticework" title="Latticework">latticework</a>. The writing materials in a scholar's private study, including elaborately carved brush holders made of stone or wood, were designed and arranged ritually to give an aesthetic appeal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199910_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199910-151">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>Connoisseurship in the late Ming period centered on these items of refined artistic taste, which provided work for art dealers and even underground scammers who themselves made imitations and false attributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199910_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199910-151">[148]</a></sup> The Jesuit <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">Matteo Ricci</a> while staying in Nanjing wrote that Chinese scam artists were ingenious at making forgeries and huge profits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998224.E2.80.935_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998224.E2.80.935-152">[149]</a></sup> However, there were guides to help the wary new connoisseur; <a href="/wiki/Liu_Tong" title="Liu Tong">Liu Tong</a> (died 1637) wrote a book printed in 1635 that told his readers how to spot fake and authentic pieces of art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998225_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998225-153">[150]</a></sup> He revealed that a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xuande" title="Xuande">Xuande era</a> (1426–1435) bronzework could be authenticated by judging its sheen; porcelain wares from the Yongle era (1402–1424) could be judged authentic by their thickness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998225.E2.80.936_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998225.E2.80.936-154">[151]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islam_during_the_Ming_dynasty" title="Islam during the Ming dynasty">Islam during the Ming dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesuit_China_missions" title="Jesuit China missions">Jesuit China missions</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Rites_Controversy" title="Chinese Rites Controversy">Chinese Rites Controversy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SFEC_BritMus_Asia_021.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="1152" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/SFEC_BritMus_Asia_021.JPG/170px-SFEC_BritMus_Asia_021.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/SFEC_BritMus_Asia_021.JPG/255px-SFEC_BritMus_Asia_021.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/SFEC_BritMus_Asia_021.JPG/340px-SFEC_BritMus_Asia_021.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SFEC_BritMus_Asia_021.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chinese glazed <a href="/wiki/Stoneware" title="Stoneware">stoneware</a> statue of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daoist" title="Daoist">Daoist</a> deity, from the Ming dynasty, 16th century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The dominant religious beliefs during the Ming dynasty were the various forms of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">Chinese folk religion</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three_Teachings" title="Three Teachings">Three Teachings</a> – <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan</a>-supported <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism" title="Tibetan Buddhism">Tibetan lamas</a> fell from favor, and the early Ming emperors particularly favored Taoism, granting its practitioners many positions in the state's ritual offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dickwang_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-dickwang-155">[152]</a></sup> The Hongwu Emperor curtailed the cosmopolitan culture of the Mongol Yuan dynasty, and the prolific Prince of Ning <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Quan" title="Zhu Quan">Zhu Quan</a> even composed one encyclopedia attacking Buddhism as a foreign "mourning cult", deleterious to the state, and another encyclopedia that subsequently joined the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist_canon" title="Taoist canon">Taoist canon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dickwang_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-dickwang-155">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Islam_in_China" title="Islam in China">Islam</a> was also well-established throughout China, with a history said to have begun with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sa%27d_ibn_Abi_Waqqas" title="Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas">Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas</a> <a href="/wiki/Islam_during_the_Tang_dynasty" title="Islam during the Tang dynasty">during the Tang dynasty</a> and strong official support <a href="/wiki/Islam_during_the_Yuan_dynasty" title="Islam during the Yuan dynasty">during the Yuan</a>. Although the Ming <a href="/wiki/Islam_during_the_Ming_dynasty" title="Islam during the Ming dynasty">sharply curtailed this support</a>, there were still several prominent Muslim figures early on, including the Hongwu Emperor's generals Chang Yuqun, <a href="/wiki/Lan_Yu_(general)" title="Lan Yu (general)">Lan Yu</a>, Ding Dexing, and <a href="/wiki/Mu_Ying" title="Mu Ying">Mu Ying</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipman199839_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipman199839-156">[153]</a></sup> as well as the Yongle Emperor's powerful eunuch <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:He_Chaozong_1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="2304" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/He_Chaozong_1.JPG/170px-He_Chaozong_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/He_Chaozong_1.JPG/255px-He_Chaozong_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/He_Chaozong_1.JPG/340px-He_Chaozong_1.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:He_Chaozong_1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bodhisattva <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manjusri" title="Manjusri">Manjusri</a> in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blanc-de-Chine" title="Blanc-de-Chine">Blanc-de-Chine</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/He_Chaozong" title="He Chaozong">He Chaozong</a>, 17th century; <a href="/wiki/Song_Yingxing" title="Song Yingxing">Song Yingxing</a> devoted an entire section of his book to the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ceramics" title="Chinese ceramics">ceramics industry</a> in the making of porcelain items like this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1965171.E2.80.932_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1965171.E2.80.932-157">[154]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The advent of the Ming was initially devastating to Christianity: in his first year, the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a> declared the eighty-year-old <a href="/wiki/Franciscans" title="Franciscans">Franciscan</a> missions among the Yuan heterodox and illegal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[155]</a></sup> The centuries-old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorianism_in_China" title="Nestorianism in China">Nestorian church</a> also disappeared. During the later Ming a new wave of Christian missionaries arrived – particularly <a href="/wiki/Jesuit_China_missions" title="Jesuit China missions">Jesuits</a> – who employed new western science and technology in their arguments for conversion. They were educated in Chinese language and culture at <a href="/wiki/St._Paul%27s_College,_Macau" title="St. Paul's College, Macau">St. Paul's College</a> on <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a> after its founding in 1579. The most influential was <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">Matteo Ricci</a>, whose "<a href="/wiki/Kunyu_Wanguo_Quantu" title="Kunyu Wanguo Quantu">Map of the Myriad Countries of the World</a>" upended <a href="/wiki/Chinese_geography" title="Chinese geography">traditional geography</a> throughout East Asia, and whose work with the convert <a href="/wiki/Xu_Guangqi" title="Xu Guangqi">Xu Guangqi</a> led to the first Chinese translation of <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Elements</a></i> in 1607. The discovery of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_stele" title="Nestorian stele">Nestorian stele</a> at <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a> in 1625 also permitted Christianity to be treated as an old and established faith, rather than as a new and dangerous cult. However, there were strong disagreements about the extent to which converts could continue to perform rituals to the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_cult#Ancient_China" title="Imperial cult">emperor</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian_temple#Worship" title="Confucian temple">Confucius</a>, or their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_ancestor_veneration" title="Chinese ancestor veneration">ancestors</a>: Ricci had been very accommodating and an attempt by his successors to backtrack from this policy led to the <a href="/wiki/Nanjing_Incident_of_1616" title="Nanjing Incident of 1616">Nanjing Incident of 1616</a>, which exiled four Jesuits to <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a> and forced the others out of public life for six years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWong196330.E2.80.932_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWong196330.E2.80.932-159">[156]</a></sup> A series of spectacular failures by the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astronomy" title="Chinese astronomy">Chinese astronomers</a> – including missing an eclipse easily computed by Xu Guangqi and <a href="/wiki/Sabatino_de_Ursis" title="Sabatino de Ursis">Sabatino de Ursis</a> – and a return by the Jesuits to presenting themselves as educated scholars in the Confucian mold<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999212_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999212-160">[157]</a></sup> restored their fortunes. However, by the end of the Ming the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominicans</a> had begun the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Rites_controversy" title="Chinese Rites controversy">Chinese Rites controversy</a> in Rome that would eventually lead to a full ban of Christianity under the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>.</p>
<p>During his mission, Ricci was also contacted in Beijing by one of the approximately 5,000 <a href="/wiki/Kaifeng_Jews" title="Kaifeng Jews">Kaifeng Jews</a> and introduced them and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_China" title="History of the Jews in China">their long history in China</a> to Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite196631.E2.80.938_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite196631.E2.80.938-161">[158]</a></sup> However, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1642_Kaifeng_flood" title="1642 Kaifeng flood">1642 flood</a> caused by Kaifeng's Ming governor devastated the community, which lost five of its twelve families, its synagogue, and most of its Torah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XX_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-XX-162">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wang_Yangming.27s_Confucianism">Wang Yangming's Confucianism</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wang-yang-ming.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="259" data-file-width="200" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3a/Wang-yang-ming.jpg" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wang-yang-ming.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Wang_Yangming" title="Wang Yangming">Wang Yangming</a> (1472–1529), considered the most influential Confucian thinker since <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the Ming dynasty, the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucian</a> doctrines of the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-bureaucrat" title="Scholar-bureaucrat">scholar</a> <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> were embraced by the court and the Chinese literati at large, although the direct line of his school was destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extermination_of_the_ten_degrees_of_kinship" title="Extermination of the ten degrees of kinship">extermination of the ten degrees of kinship</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fang_Xiaoru" title="Fang Xiaoru">Fang Xiaoru</a> in 1402.</p>
<p>The Ming scholar most influential upon subsequent generations, however, was <a href="/wiki/Wang_Yangming" title="Wang Yangming">Wang Yangming</a> (1472–1529), whose teachings were attacked in his own time for their similarity to <a href="/wiki/Zen" title="Zen">Chan Buddhism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006282_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006282-163">[160]</a></sup> Building upon Zhu Xi's concept of the "extension of knowledge" (<span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">理學</span> or <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">格物致知</span>), gaining understanding through careful and rational investigation of things and events, Wang argued that universal concepts would appear in the minds of anyone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006281_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006281-164">[161]</a></sup> Therefore, he claimed that anyone – no matter their pedigree or education – could become as wise as <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a> had been and that their writings were not sources of truth but merely guides that might have flaws when carefully examined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006281.E2.80.93282_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006281.E2.80.93282-165">[162]</a></sup> A peasant with a great deal of experience and intelligence would then be wiser than an official who had memorized the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Classics" title="Five Classics">Classics</a> but not experienced the real world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006281.E2.80.93282_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006281.E2.80.93282-165">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservative_reaction">Conservative reaction</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Confucius_on_his_way_to_Luoyang.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="469" data-file-width="423" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Confucius_on_his_way_to_Luoyang.jpg/220px-Confucius_on_his_way_to_Luoyang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Confucius_on_his_way_to_Luoyang.jpg/330px-Confucius_on_his_way_to_Luoyang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/64/Confucius_on_his_way_to_Luoyang.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Confucius_on_his_way_to_Luoyang.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Ming dynasty print drawing of <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> on his way to the <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a> capital of <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-bureaucrat" title="Scholar-bureaucrat">scholar-bureaucrats</a> were wary of Wang's heterodoxy, the increasing number of his disciples while he was still in office, and his overall socially rebellious message. To curb his influence, he was often sent out to deal with military affairs and rebellions far away from the capital. Yet his ideas penetrated mainstream Chinese thought and spurred new interest in Taoism and Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006282_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006282-163">[160]</a></sup> Furthermore, people began to question the validity of the social hierarchy and the idea that the scholar should be above the farmer. Wang Yangming's disciple and salt-mine worker Wang Gen gave lectures to commoners about pursuing education to improve their lives, while his follower He Xinyin (<span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">何心隱</span>) challenged the elevation and emphasis of the family in Chinese society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006282_163-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006282-163">[160]</a></sup> His contemporary <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Zhi_(Ming_dynasty)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Li Zhi (Ming dynasty) (page does not exist)">Li Zhi</a> even taught that women were the intellectual equals of men and should be given a better education; both Li and He eventually died in prison, jailed on charges of spreading "dangerous ideas".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006283_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbreyWalthallPalais2006283-166">[163]</a></sup> Yet these "dangerous ideas" of educating women had long been embraced by some mothers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999158_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999158-167">[164]</a></sup> and by <a href="/wiki/Courtesan" title="Courtesan">courtesans</a> who were as literate and skillful in calligraphy, painting, and poetry as their male guests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998230_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998230-168">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>The liberal views of Wang Yangming were opposed by the <a href="/wiki/Censorate" title="Censorate">Censorate</a> and by the <a href="/wiki/Donglin_Academy" title="Donglin Academy">Donglin Academy</a>, re-established in 1604. These conservatives wanted a revival of orthodox Confucian ethics. Conservatives such as Gu Xiancheng (1550–1612) argued against Wang's idea of innate moral knowledge, stating that this was simply a legitimization for unscrupulous behavior such as greedy pursuits and personal gain. These two strands of Confucian thought, hardened by Chinese scholars' notions of obligation towards their mentors, developed into pervasive factionalism among the ministers of state, who used any opportunity to impeach members of the other faction from court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999213_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999213-169">[166]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_and_rural_life">Urban and rural life</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B-ChinesischeLackdose.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1979" data-file-width="2339" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/B-ChinesischeLackdose.JPG/220px-B-ChinesischeLackdose.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/B-ChinesischeLackdose.JPG/330px-B-ChinesischeLackdose.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/B-ChinesischeLackdose.JPG/440px-B-ChinesischeLackdose.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B-ChinesischeLackdose.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Ming dynasty red <a href="/wiki/Lacquerware" title="Lacquerware">lacquer box</a> with <a href="/wiki/Carved_lacquer" title="Carved lacquer">intricate carving</a> of people in the countryside, surrounded by a floral border design.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wang Gen was able to give philosophical lectures to many commoners from different regions because – following the trend already apparent in the Song dynasty – communities in Ming society were becoming less isolated as the distance between market towns was shrinking. Schools, descent groups, religious associations, and other local voluntary organizations were increasing in number and allowing more contact between educated men and local villagers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999206_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999206-170">[167]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Spence" title="Jonathan Spence">Jonathan Spence</a> writes that the distinction between what was town and country was blurred in Ming China, since suburban areas with farms were located just outside and in some cases within the walls of a city. Not only was the blurring of town and country evident, but also of socioeconomic class in the traditional four occupations (Chinese: 士農工商), since artisans sometimes worked on farms in peak periods, and farmers often traveled into the city to find work during times of dearth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199913_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199913-171">[168]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Minghuang%27s_Journey_to_Sichuan,_Freer_Gallery_of_Art.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Emperor_Minghuang%27s_Journey_to_Sichuan%2C_Freer_Gallery_of_Art.jpg/220px-Emperor_Minghuang%27s_Journey_to_Sichuan%2C_Freer_Gallery_of_Art.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Emperor_Minghuang%27s_Journey_to_Sichuan%2C_Freer_Gallery_of_Art.jpg/330px-Emperor_Minghuang%27s_Journey_to_Sichuan%2C_Freer_Gallery_of_Art.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Emperor_Minghuang%27s_Journey_to_Sichuan%2C_Freer_Gallery_of_Art.jpg/440px-Emperor_Minghuang%27s_Journey_to_Sichuan%2C_Freer_Gallery_of_Art.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Minghuang%27s_Journey_to_Sichuan,_Freer_Gallery_of_Art.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Emperor Minghuang's Journey to Sichuan</i>, a Ming dynasty painting after <a href="/wiki/Qiu_Ying" title="Qiu Ying">Qiu Ying</a> (1494–1552).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A variety of occupations could be chosen or inherited from a father's line of work. This would include – but was not limited to – coffinmakers, ironworkers and blacksmiths, tailors, cooks and noodle-makers, retail merchants, tavern, teahouse, or winehouse managers, shoemakers, seal cutters, pawnshop owners, brothel heads, and merchant bankers engaging in a proto-banking system involving notes of exchange.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199920_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199920-66">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199912.E2.80.933_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199912.E2.80.933-172">[169]</a></sup> Virtually every town had a <a href="/wiki/Brothel" title="Brothel">brothel</a> where female and male prostitutes could be had.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998229.2C_232_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998229.2C_232-173">[170]</a></sup> Male catamites fetched a higher price than female concubines since <a href="/wiki/Pederasty" title="Pederasty">pederasty</a> with a teenage boy was seen as a mark of elite status, regardless of <a href="/wiki/Sodomy" title="Sodomy">sodomy</a> being repugnant to sexual norms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998232.E2.80.933_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998232.E2.80.933-174">[171]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Public_bathing" title="Public bathing">Public bathing</a> became much more common than in earlier periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchafer195657_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchafer195657-175">[172]</a></sup> Urban shops and retailers sold a variety of goods such as <a href="/wiki/Joss_paper" title="Joss paper">special paper money</a> to burn at ancestral sacrifices, specialized luxury goods, headgear, fine cloth, teas, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199912.E2.80.933_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199912.E2.80.933-172">[169]</a></sup> Smaller communities and townships too poor or scattered to support shops and artisans obtained their goods from periodic market fairs and traveling peddlers. A small township also provided a place for simple schooling, news and gossip, matchmaking, religious festivals, traveling theater groups, tax collection, and bases of famine relief distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199913_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199913-171">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>Farming villagers in the north spent their days harvesting crops like wheat and millet, while farmers south of the <a href="/wiki/Huai_River" title="Huai River">Huai River</a> engaged in intensive rice cultivation and had lakes and ponds where ducks and fish could be raised. The cultivation of mulberry trees for silkworms and tea bushes could be found mostly south of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangzi_River" title="Yangzi River">Yangzi River</a>; even further south <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> and citrus were grown as basic crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199913_171-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199913-171">[168]</a></sup> Some people in the mountainous southwest made a living by selling lumber from hard bamboo. Besides cutting down trees to sell wood, the poor also made a living by turning wood into charcoal, and by burning <a href="/wiki/Oyster" title="Oyster">oyster</a> shells to make <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_lime" title="Agricultural lime">lime</a> and fired pots, and weaving mats and baskets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199895_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199895-176">[173]</a></sup> In the north traveling by horse and carriage was most common, while in the south the myriad of rivers, canals, and lakes provided cheap and easy water transport. Although the south had the characteristic of the wealthy landlord and tenant farmers, there were on average many more owner-cultivators north of the Huai River due to harsher climate, living not far above subsistence level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence199914_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence199914-177">[174]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_and_technology_in_China" title="History of science and technology in China">History of science and technology in China</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_inventions" title="List of Chinese inventions">List of Chinese inventions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_discoveries" title="List of Chinese discoveries">List of Chinese discoveries</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="794" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg/220px-Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg/330px-Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg/440px-Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The puddling process of smelting iron <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> to make <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a>, with the right illustration displaying men working a <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a>, from the <i><a href="/wiki/Song_Yingxing" title="Song Yingxing">Tiangong Kaiwu</a></i> encyclopedia, 1637.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1036" data-file-width="1454" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif/220px-Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif/330px-Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif/440px-Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the known world by <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a>: <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> at the top, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceylon" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a> at the upper right and <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> along the bottom. Sailing directions and distances are marked using <i>zhenlu</i> (Chinese: 針路) or compass route.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Compared to the flourishing of <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty#Technology.2C_science.2C_and_engineering" title="Song dynasty">science and technology in the Song dynasty</a>, the Ming dynasty perhaps saw fewer advancements in science and technology compared to the pace of discovery in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>. In fact, key advances in Chinese science in the late Ming were spurred by contact with Europe. In 1626 <a href="/wiki/Johann_Adam_Schall_von_Bell" title="Johann Adam Schall von Bell">Johann Adam Schall von Bell</a> wrote the first Chinese treatise on the <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>, the <i>Yuanjingshuo</i> (<i>Far Seeing Optic Glass</i>); in 1634 the <a href="/wiki/Chongzhen_Emperor" title="Chongzhen Emperor">Chongzhen Emperor</a> acquired the telescope of the late <a href="/wiki/Johann_Schreck" title="Johann Schreck">Johann Schreck</a> (1576–1630).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1959444.E2.80.935_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1959444.E2.80.935-178">[175]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentric</a> model of the solar system was rejected by the Catholic missionaries in China, but <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>'s ideas slowly trickled into China starting with the Polish Jesuit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Boym" title="Michael Boym">Michael Boym</a> (1612–59) in 1627, Adam Schall von Bell's treatise in 1640, and finally <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Edkins" title="Joseph Edkins">Joseph Edkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Wylie_(missionary)" title="Alexander Wylie (missionary)">Alex Wylie</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Fryer_Thomas_Keane" title="John Fryer Thomas Keane">John Fryer</a> in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1959444.E2.80.937_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1959444.E2.80.937-179">[176]</a></sup> Catholic Jesuits in China would promote <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Copernican</a> theory at court, yet at the same time embrace the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemaic</a> system in their writing; it was not until 1865 that Catholic missionaries in China sponsored the heliocentric model as their Protestant peers did.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWong1963p._31.2C_footnote_1_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWong1963p._31.2C_footnote_1-180">[177]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Shen_Kuo" title="Shen Kuo">Shen Kuo</a> (1031–95) and <a href="/wiki/Guo_Shoujing" title="Guo Shoujing">Guo Shoujing</a> (1231–1316) had laid the basis for <a href="/wiki/History_of_trigonometry" title="History of trigonometry">trigonometry</a> in China, another important work in Chinese trigonometry would not be published again until 1607 with the efforts of Xu Guangqi and Matteo Ricci.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1959110_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1959110-181">[178]</a></sup> Ironically, some inventions which had their origins in ancient China were reintroduced to China from Europe during the late Ming; for example, the <a href="/wiki/Field_mill_(carriage)" title="Field mill (carriage)">field mill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1965255.E2.80.937_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1965255.E2.80.937-182">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calendar" title="Chinese calendar">Chinese calendar</a> was in need of reform since it inadequately measured the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_year" title="Solar year">solar year</a> at 365 ¼ days, giving an error of 10 min and 14 sec a year or roughly a full day every 128 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuttner1975166_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuttner1975166-183">[180]</a></sup> Although the Ming had adopted <a href="/wiki/Guo_Shoujing" title="Guo Shoujing">Guo Shoujing</a>'s <i>Shoushi</i> calendar of 1281, which was just as accurate as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gregorian_Calendar" title="Gregorian Calendar">Gregorian Calendar</a>, the Ming Directorate of Astronomy failed to periodically readjust it; this was perhaps due to their lack of expertise since their offices had become hereditary in the Ming and the Statutes of the Ming prohibited private involvement in astronomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878-184">[181]</a></sup> A sixth-generation descendant of the Hongxi Emperor, the "Prince" <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Zaiyu" title="Zhu Zaiyu">Zhu Zaiyu</a> (1536–611), submitted a proposal to fix the calendar in 1595, but the ultra-conservative astronomical commission rejected it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuttner1975166_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuttner1975166-183">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878-184">[181]</a></sup> This was the same Zhu Zaiyu who discovered the system of tuning known as <a href="/wiki/Equal_temperament" title="Equal temperament">equal temperament</a>, a discovery made simultaneously by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a> (1548–1620) in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuttner1975166.E2.80.937_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuttner1975166.E2.80.937-185">[182]</a></sup> In addition to publishing his works on music, he was able to publish his findings on the calendar in 1597.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878_184-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878-184">[181]</a></sup> A year earlier, the memorial of Xing Yunlu suggesting a calendar improvement was rejected by the Supervisor of the Astronomical Bureau due to the law banning private practice of astronomy;
